WASHINGTON, Jan. 14.-The United
States Supreme Court to-day announced
Its'declsion in the case of C. W. F. Neely,
charged with embezzlement of the public
funds of Cuba while acting as financial
agent of the department of posts of that
Island, holding that Neely is subject to
extradition. ' ,
Justice Harlan handed down the court's
decision, which .was unanimous, and im
mediately after the decision was rendered
an order, was issued requiring that the
mandate In tho case be Issued. at once.
Entering then upon his reasoning in the
case, Justice Harlan said that there was
no dispute that on the 6th of June. 1900,
when the act under which this proceed
ing is brought became a law. Cuba was
"under the control of the United States"
and "occupied by this Government."
"The court,", he said, "will take judicial'
notice that such were at the date named
and are now the relations between this
country and Cuba. So that the applica
bility of .the above act to the present case

deemed a foreign country. or territory. >
"We do not think this question 1 at all
difficult of solution if regard be had to
the avowed objects Intended to be accom
plished by the -war with Spain ' and by
the military occupation of that island.?
Announcing the ' court's ' conclusion on
the status of Cuba, Justice Harlan said:
"The facts above detailed make it clear
that Cuba is foreign territory within the
meaning of the act of June 6. 1900. ' It can
not be regarded in any constitutional, le
gal or international; sense apart of the
territory of the United States. While by
the act of April 25/ 1898, war was declared
between this V country ' and Spain, the
President was directed and empowered to
use our entire land and. naval forces, ; as
well as the militia of the several ' States,
to such extent as, was, necessary to carry
the act into effect, that authorization was
not ; . for ' the - purpose â¢ of making : Cuba an
integral part of the United [ States; Â¦ but
the purpose; only of compelling the reiin
quishment"; by s Spain of its and
the withdrawal of its 'forces , from- Cuba
and Cuban '.waters. The ; legislative, and
executive branches of the Government, oy
the Joint resolution of April 20, 1898, ex
pressly^disclaimed any purpose to exer- :
cise sovereignty, jurisdiction or .control
over ' Cuba, '. 'except . for ; the pacification
thereof,' and. asserts the 'determination of
the United -States," that objects being, ac

LELAND STANFORD JUNIOR UNIVERSITY.
Department cf History-
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. Cal., Jan. 14, 1901.
President David S. Jordan. Stanford University, Cal.â Dear Sir: Your
letter accepting my resignation is received. Of course I am well aware
that the rights of the large number of students now registered In my
classes are involved in fixing the time of my resignation. Therefore,
since I am being dismissed from a life position on. the 'alleged ground
at discourtesy to the authorities of the university. It neemad to me
but fair that you should take the responsibility of saying whether I
should remain to thÂ« end of the year. You decline to take, that re
sponsibility, and so leave me but one safe and dignified course.
I should have been willing to remain to the. close of the year for
the sake of my students, could I have felt sure that by "harmonious
co-operation" you mean a faithful and free performance of academic
duty according to the spirit of the original Implied contract under
which I have thus far labored. I have not changed J my attitude
toward the university or toward my professorial duties. I am. only
protesting against revolutionary proceedings. The vital point of the
whole present incident is a question of free speech. Therefore ; 1^ am
not willing to pledfre myself . in advance to abide by the uncertain
.. interrretaUon of the ambiguous pbre*e:.'.'*'.e>Mnjl* your ,*Â»e.Ungs In the
matter 'prevent harmonious co-operation until that time." Hence I
wish my resignation to take effect at once. Very truly yours, ,
GEORGE E. HOWARD.
Professor George Elliott Howard is one of the most gifted'
historians in America. He graduated from the University of
Nebraska in 1S76, took his A. M. in 1879 and his Ph.D. in 1S94.
He was a student in history and jurisprudence at the universi
ties of Munich and Paris from 1876. to. 1878. From 1879 to 1891 he
was professor of history in the University of Nebraska. He
also acted as secretary of the Nebraska State Historical Soci
ety from 1SS5 to 1891.
When Stanford University -opened Dr. Howard was the
first man chosen for a position on the faculty of the new in
stitution by President Jordan. Among his students Dr. How
ard Is looked to as an Inspiring leader In intellectual pursuits.
President Jordan, speaking of his work to a Stanford graduate,
said: "We have many good teachers at Stanford, but Dr. How- \
ard comes the nearest to being- a really great teacher. He is
in many respects an ideal teacher."
Professor Howard has been at Stanford ever since the doors
of the university were thrown open. During this time he has
given his best thought and work to the Institution, with the
result that his department ha3 made Stanford famous. So
far has his judgment been trusted that ho was allowed to draw
the plans for the new building which is being erected for the
use of the departments of history, English and economics. He
it was who had planned the academy of history, economics and
sociology for graduate work which would occupy the Stanford
residence in San Francisco.

He said that Professor Ross was a martyr to a principle
which cannot be sacrificed and that his forced retirement would
work immeasurable evil to the university. He declared that in
the retirement of Professor Ross a serious blow had been
etruck at the frectlom of speech and the consequences must be
as far-reaching as they were deplorable. In conclusion he ut
tered these notabl? words, which are supposed to have been
the most objectionable to the university authorities:
At the time the address was given It created a profound
sensation and was the subject of much comment outside of
college circles as well as within. The impassioned outburst
Â¦was before Dr. Howard's class In "French Revolution." Stu
dents that heard him will long remember how intensely sin
cere their impassioned professor was that morning. He opened
his address by declaring that he could not confine himself to
Voltaire and the Intellectual bondage of the past century when
the present vime presented an incident of bigotry and intoler
ence equally as flagrant.
The causes leading up to Dr. Howards enforced res
ignation are wholly an outgrowth of the dismissal of Dr. E. A.
Ross from the department of sociology last November. Dr.
Howard was one of the most outspoken of several of the Stan
ford faculty who regarded Mrs. Stanford's interference as a
blow at freedom of speech and academic liberty. On Novem
ber 15. the day after Dr. Ross' resignation had been accepted.
Dr. Howard delivered the address which aroused President
Jordan to call for either his apology or resignation. Professor
Howard was a close personal friend of Dr. Ross and^ shared
something of the same independence and intellectual freedom
which characterized the distinguished economist.

which Â¦ has been fought out once' be
fore in - .the Legislature and â¢ which

The report will ,coine before' the
Legislature in. the form of: bills' em
bodying the recommendations., made' by
the commission. ; Among them is one that
chaiflfcs the law relating to contempt
committed outside of the presence of the
court so as to allow an appeal from a
Judgment In contempt. Under the present
law there is ho appeal, but the judgment
in contempt may be reviewed by the high
er court upon a writ of certiorari, which,
however, runs only as to the jurisdiction
of the lower court. The difference be
tween the writ and the appeal Is that the
writ is a quick remedy, the appeal a slow
one, and that in the case of an appeal
the party enjoined may put up a bond
and continue the work he is supposed to
have stopped.
This matter, of appeal in contempt pro
ceedings was brought up in a report of
a Code Commission four years ago, the
source of It being a faction of the hy
draulic miners. The hydraulickers under
the. law were v enjoined from hydraulic
mining excepr under certain conditions,
on the ground that their work was dam
aging the farms and rivers below them.
It was pointed out at the time that if
they were allowed to appeal from Judg
ments in contempt and the then existing
conditions they could tie up a Judgment
..for two or three years before the higher
court, and in the meantime continue to
work their mines in defiance of the in
junction and the fubsequent contempt
proceedings. .
When the proposition to change appeals
first came up before the Legislature there
was a storm of protest from the valley
men, who saw in It an. attempt to work
the hydraulic mines despite the legal pro
hibition. , There was not then nor has
there been since any" Indorsement of such
a proposition from the Miners' Associa
tion, for it is the rule of that organiza
tion not to countenance any but legal
mining-. The proposition was killed and
it has lain dormant, until now It appears
again in one of the three voluminous bills
which are based on the report of the Code
Commission. â¢ â¢ Â¦ Â¦Â¦Â¦
The opponents of the , clause declare
that, without going Into the matter, an
amendment to the code providing appeals
in contempt cases Is new legislation, and
is in no .way within the province of the
Code Commission. It is claimed that the
Code Â¦ Commission can go no further than
a revision of the codes, and that it can
not add anything new to them. The pro
vision is. deemed a bad one, and several
Senators have announced their determina
tion, to vote. against all three bills on the
venture that 4 if the appeal In contempt
has been brought In- there must be much
that is objectionable. . .

LICENSES FOR

practice of acquiring a residence In this
State for the purpose of"taking advantage
of the divorce laws of California, and It
also knocks out the year's probation
which has caused so much controversy.'
Senator Nutt's bill provides that no di
vorce shall be granted for any cause aris
ing prior to the residence of the com- #
plainant or defendant In. this State, which*
was not a ground for divorce in the State
where the cause arose; also that no per
son shall be entitled to a divorce in this
State for any cause arising in this State,
who has not had actual residence in this
State for at least one year before the
commencement of the suit, with a bona
fide Intention of making this State his or
her permanent home. For. causes arising
outside of this State bona fide residence
of two years, with the intention of re
maining here, is required.
The bill requires personal service of
summons, or service to the complete sat
isfaction of the court, and it Is further
provided -that none but personal service
shall be accepted until a diligent search
for the defendant for at least six months
from the commencement of the suit shall
have been proved. The bill also prohibits
the granting of decrees solely upon de
fault or upon admission in the pleadings,
except after hearing before court.
It is provided that after divorce either
party may marry again, no time limit be
ing set forth, but there is a restriction
made in cases where the notice has been
by publication only, and the defendant
has not appeared.' In that case six
months must elapse before the decree be
comes operative or effective, when it shall
become final.

The exhibits of this State were collected with
the utmost care; were duly forwarded and
handsomely installed In five "different depart
ments and pavilions at the Paris Exposition,
namely: Horticulture. . agriculture, mining,
forestry and fishery: these being the five groups Â¦
in which the California commission principally
exerted all their energies.
This Ftate was allotted six Jurors, one for
fach of these departments, and the different
irroups were divided into 1O.> different classes,
of which California had a *i>lend!ri representa
tion In twenty-four of them. This Ftate had
mi -elegant display, consisting of a full line of
all varieties of dried, canned, conserved, freah
end citrus fruits, raletns, nuts, wines, olive
oils, cereals, wool, woods, leather, perfumery,
mapsi education, social economy and varied In
dustries.
The following summary of awards speaks for
itself: Out of a total of 577 exhibitors from this
State. &6K received awards at the. hands of the
international Jury. Thus it will be seen that
of all our exhibitors 98 per cent recelvei
awards, while only 60 per cent of the world's
exhibitors received any mention. Including
these awards the State of California secured a
crand prize for Its collective exhibits in each
of the five departments stove mentioned, and
five additional gold medals on separate exhib
its in these and other departments. California
exhibits were distributed in the different de
partments and grouped with similar exhibits
of the world. Attractive signs were placed con
rplcuously over California exhibits directing the
visitors to our State display, which had been
artistically arranged, and the name of Cali
fornia soon became a household word.
Another feature of the Commissioners' work
and which brought great prominence to the
fctate was the â¢ headquarters bureau . of in
formation, collective exhibits and offices, main
tained at R Place de l'Opera. the very center
of the most populous quarter of Paris, which
rirew thousands of visitors of nil nationalities
peeking information of the resources of our
ftate. It can be stated this was the only State
in the Union that maintained such headquarters
outside of the exposition.
An efficient corps of assistants familiar with
the resources of our great State and conversant
with tho French. German, Spanish and Italian
languages were always on hand at these head
quarters to furnish any Information required,
also to assist Callforniana in and about Paris;
also to furnish them with data as to where our
exhibits at the exposition could be found and
give them all information while In Paris.
There wu also maintained a commercial bu
reau, whose function It was to distribute
throughout Europe albums and maps printed
in French, English and German. Illustrative of
< the resources of this State. It is pleasing to
note in this connection that over 300 answers
were received from Boards of Trade and Cham
bers of Commerce acknowledging receipt of
these maps and albums, and thanking the com
mission for their courtesy in the matter and
also assuring the commission that the map*
would be prominently displayed on their walls.
The commission has fully demonstrated that
many products of this State are now In. the
markets of France and that by further enter
prise of our producers many others can be suc
cessfully and profitably sold there.
Of the $130,000 appropriated by our Legisla
ture, which was turned over to the commission,
about $116,000 will have been expended when
this commission shall have finished their, work,
thus leaving a balance of about $14,000 to be
turned bark to the State treasury. The com
mteshm will render a full and complete report
of all' its proceedings from the organization to
the closing of Its affairs, so that the people of
this State may have a full report of how the
money was expended and the affairs managed.
All of which is respectfully submitted.

MENTO, Jan. 14.â The inaugura
tion of a President could not have
brought a larger crowd to the As
sembly chamber this afternoon
than the one which gathered there to see
the vote "of the State Electors cast for
William McKinley and Theodore Roose
velt for President and Vice President of
the Unltea States. It was a new thing to
make of the simple act a public and im
pressive ceremony, but there was no doubt
of the success of the experiment nor of
the lasting impression which it'made.
Flags were draped around the galleries
and above and behind the.Speaker's desk.
Palms and evergreens were clustered be
low the flags, around the gallery rails and
along the walls, and from the central
chandelier hung pennants of the national
colors, moving gracefully over the throng
that filled every seat and every corner ot
the big room. ..â¢ â¢ . . .
At the. hour set the. crowd around -the
entrance was parted, and, led by the Gov- ;
ernor, the official? who were to take. part
in the programme entered, the audience
cheering the executive as he made his
way up the aisle. Following him were
Colonel George Stone, chairman of the
Republican State Central . Committee;
Jacob H. Neff.' Lieutenant Governor, of
the State; Thomas Flint Jr. President
pro tem. of the Senate, and C.- W. Pen
dleton. Speaker of the Assembly. They
took their places behind the Speaker's
desk. Secretary of Statp Charles F. Curry
followed, and then came the nine Electors,
who took seats at the clerk's desk. Col
onel Stone rapped for order, opened the
ceremonies with a brief speech and then
introduced Governor Gage, who spoke as
follows:
Mr. Chairman, Blectors, Ladles and Gentle
men: The people of the great State of Cali
fornia cast their vote on the 6th day of last
November by no uncertain majority for two
distlDtruished citizens for President, and Vice
President. While they voted Indirectly for the
men whom thpy wished to occupy those high
offices, it becomes your duty," electors, to carry
out the will of the people who elected you by
a direct vote for William McKinley, for Presi
dent, and Theodore Roosevelt, for Vice Presi
dent, n .
It is pleasant to recalVthe historical fact
that while the framers or the constitution
feared to give the people the power to Bay
directly who should fill the higheet offices ot
the nation, no electors since, perhaps, 1S04
have gone contrary to the wish of the' people
in casting their ballots. The constitution does
not bind you to vote except as your own Â¦wish
may dictate, but custom- has decreed that the
will of the people shall be respected and your
ballots to-day will doubtless be. cast as the
will of the people expressed at the polls de
mands. â¢ .

WASHINGTON, Jan. 14.-The Postal
Commission of the House and Senate,
which has been investigating postal mat
ters for the. lost two years, made its re
port to-day. The commission reports in
favor of excluding from the second class
mail rate â first, books, whether bound or
unbound; second, newspapers and periodi
cals unsealed sent by a news agent to an
other news agent or returned to the pub
lisher; third, sample copies of newspapers
above a certain small fixed proportion of
the circulation. The commission also
unanimously reports against the continu
ance of the service of transmitting mails
In pneumatic tubes under present â¢con
ditions. The commission unanimously re
ports that neither a change in letter post
age nor the establishment of a system
of parcels post is practicable under exist
ing cond'*'ons of revenue and expendi
ture, eve. '* otherwise desirable, upon
which no opinion is expressed.
Upon the question of railway mall pay
there are five reports: First, a report
signed by Senators Wolcott and Allison,
which declares that the present rate of
railway mall pay Is not excessive and
ought not to be changed, except by the
discontinuance of the appropriations for
special facilities, which It declares should
be discontinued.
Second, a report signed by Mr. Load,

j or more guests were forced to make a
j journey to a city as quiet at this time ot

j from these unusual circumstances. The
j cnurcn was decorated with gorgeous lav
1 ishness, but was only about half filled

green and white in many charming com
binations were the prevailing colors, the
distinctive flowers being the Glolre de ia
Reine begonias, the Golden Gate roses
with white azaleas and the specimen
palms.
The center aisle was a lane of begonias
and asparagus, standards eight feet high
being trimmed with the feathery foliage
cf the latter at regular intervals from the
entrance to the chancel. Baskets of deli
cate pink flowers depended from the
standards. Specimen palms thirty feet
high towered high above the altar. Inter
spersed among the foliage which banked
them were great clusters of white azaleas
and medallions of Golden Gate roses. On
the altar was an immense cross of As
cension lilies.

SAX JOSE, Jan. 14.â Mayor Martin has
Instituted a crusade against saloons that
women are allowed to frequent. This
caaif as a sensation to the City Council at
its meeting this morning, when his Honor
presented a message closing side entrances
to saloons. The move was brought about
by the assault upon and brutal treatment
leceived by Marie Staten at the hands of
three your.g roughs a few davs ago.
In hie message Mayor Martin says that
a woman was recently Induced to enter
a saloon, and was assaulted, beaten and
robbed, and in the wide publicity of the
fact the good name of San Jose for peace
end order has been called into question.
In the past few months he has noticed
considerable increase in these side doors
and ladies' entrances and their unmis
takable signs of invitation to the un
wary, and that the preservation of the
morals of the unsophisticated demands
their suppression.
The matter was referred to the Ordin
ance Committee.
The police, under the direction of Chief
Kidwartl. are arresting all dissolute char
acters Uith the hope of freeing the town
of touts, "fiends" and degraded women.
Twenty-five warrants were sworn to to
day charging various persons with va
grancy, and about sixty more will be is-
Fued to-morrow. As soon as the culprits
are arrested they are taken into court
where they are given a chance to either
leave Mwn or i>e prosecuted. All agree to
leave. In this way it is hoped to free the
c:ty of /his undesirable class.

PARIS, Jan. 14.â In the Chamber of Dep
uties to-day the Government was Inter
pellated by Marcel Zambat. Radical So
cialist, on the "interference of the Pope
in the domestic affairs of France," as
shown in the recent letter of the Pope to
Cardinal Richard, the Archbishop of
Paris, deallnsr with the proposed bill of
the Premier, 34. "Waideck-Kousseau,
known as the law of associations, and
aiming; at the suppression of religious
communities, in whicn the Pontiff detend
Â«d the religious orders. M. Za-mbat asked
the Government to protest against this
letter.
AL Ribot, the former Premier, defended
the Pope, mho, he said, was entitled to
raise his voice when h.e thought religious
Interests were in Deril.
The statement called forth cheers from
the liightists and Centrists.
The entire session was spent in discuss
ing the interpellation of St. Zambat, the
debate closing with a declaration by M.
\Valdeck-Roussea.u. who said the Govern
ment saw no threat in the Pope's letter
as to the missions in the East.
"The letter of his Holiness." said the
Premier, "simply said if the French mis
sionaries disappeared they would be re-
Viaced by foreign missionaries.
"The Government will not tolerate the
organization of resistance apanist the
laws of the country. No Interference on
the part of the Pope will be allowed in the
law of associations when it is voted upon.
The Pope has rights as the spiritual bead
of Catholics, but the state also has rights
written In the concordat, which it will see
respected."
This declaration rallied around the Gov
ernment all the Leftists and Centrists,
who voted a poll of the Premier's declara
tions by 4Â£9 to 95, and confidence In his
firmness to assure the defense of the
ricrhts of the state.
The Chamber then adopted a resolution
embodying both paragraphs.
Other extremist motions, one proposing
the separation of church and state and
enother proposing to denounce the con
cordat, were rejected by overwhelming
majorities.
The Chamber was crowded with Deputies
end notable visitors !n the expectation
that the debate on the law of associations
would be begim. To-day's resolution has
greatly elated the Ministerialists, who de
clare that it presages victory for the law
cf associations.

J A. Filcher. secretary of the State
Board of Trade, addressed the convention.
He said that If the prejudices of the
French could be brushed aside France,
which was a nation of wine drinkers,
would offer a great market for California
wine. It was a fact not generally known.
vBaid Mr. Filcher, that in France good
wines commanded as high a price as they
did In this country, and the masses drank
the vilest product imaginable. He dwelt
upon the good results of the exhibits of
California wines and fruits at Paris, and
said that markets were being found for
our products In all parts of the globe.
The afternoon session closed with a talk
on "Immigration" by "William E. Smythe,
vice president of the "Water and Forest
Association. Mr. Sraythe defined the
classes of immigration which came to Cal
ifornia and suggested means of keeping It
here. The address evoked a general dis
cussion.

Club to-njght the Sacramento Chamber
of Commerce held open house to the dele
gates in attendance upon the joint conven
tion of the Sacramento and San Joaquin
"Valleys Development Associations, which
was called to order this afternoon. There
were present many members of the Legis
lature, and the hours between 8 and 11
were pleasantly and profitably spent. The
serious work of the Joint convention will
not take place until to-morrow, although
a beginning was made at this afternoon's
brief session.

Will S. Green of Colusa who said that it
was within the power of the present gen
eration to lay 'n the Sacramento and San
Joaquin valleys the foundation of a vast
empire. Arthur R. Brlggs of Fresno was

WASHINGTON, Jan. 14.â A letter re
ceived here from a naval officer indicates
that Captain Hall, accused of cowardice
at Peking, is a victim of petticoat diplo
macy. Captain Hall was in command of
the marines from July 3, when Captain
Meyers was wounded, until the siege of
Peitlng ended.
"I believe the charges," said the naval
officer, "grew out or the fact that the
marines would not take on* their hats to
the. wife of the First Secretary. There
was much feeling on the part of non-com
missioned officers and privates against the
Kirst Secretary and his wife and Justly
in my opinion.
"It -seems that the Chinese steward of
Mr. Conger told our marines that thero
had been a row between Mrs. Conger antl
the wife of the First Secretary becau.se
Mrs. Conger wanted to send the marines
on the wall some of the canned goods
which the marines had themselves cap
tured and turned into the general store
in the British Legation. Mrs. First Sec
retary refused to send anything, and so,
according to the Chinese steward, the
ladies stopped speaking to one another.
"Our marines thus got the impression
that Mrs. Conger was their friend and
that the' wife of the First Secretary was
not, and the marines used to stand at at
tention when Mrs. Conger passed in at the
Legation gate, while they did not when
the First Secretary's wife entered.
"One day during the siege, as the wife
of the Secretary passed through the Lega
tion gate from the British Legation and
the marines did not stand at attention, she
took them to task, calling them 'bums
and tramps' and saying that they wero
not fit to be soldiers.
"Passing into the Legation grounds and
seeing Captain Hall asleep in a chair she"
awakened him by calling, 'Captain Hall,
your men do not salute me. They are
Boers.'

SANTA CRUZ, Jan. 14.â Fire at 4 o'clock
this morning destroyed the seven-room
cottage on West Sycamore street belong
ing to Alba Wyman. One evening., last
week a party of Rebekahs returning from
Soquel passed this dwelling just in time
to save It from destruction. The interior
had been saturated with kerosene and a
flre had been started. This morning's con
flagration was started in the same man
ner. John Rhodes, who first discovered
it. found the whole interior a blazing fur
nace.
Mr. Wyrnan, who owned the residence,
is an old man and resides next door. He
sleeps on the opposite side of the house
and is very deaf. He did not hear the
crackling of the flames and Rhodes broke
into the house and awakened him. The
s._e of the house he occupied had already
caught fire/ but the flames were extin
guished after it had been badly scarred.

BOXING CONTESTS LIMITED.
Supervisors Declare for Only .One
Professional Exhibition a Mpnth.
At a meeting of the Supervisors yester
day the board passed finally an amended
ordinance regrflating amateur boxing con
tests so that only one exhibition shall be
given in any one month by any club, and
then only in the gymnasium of the club
under whose auspices it is held. The or
dinance also limits the number of profes
sional boxing exhibitions to one eacn
month, under which rule the dubs will
have to take their turn.

Jack Gleason Defeats "Billy" Ken-
BRILLIANT HANDBALL
PLAYED BY AMATEURS
nedy in a Sensational
Game.
Interest in the handball tournament now
on at the Olympic Club is on the increase.
The attendance at the games last night
was very large and the spectators waxed
enthusiastic over fierce rallies and clever
play*. The best exhibition of tha evenm*
was put up* by â¢â¢Billy" Kennedy ana
"Jack" Gleason. The latter won two
straight, but his victory did not come easy
to him. Neither Gleason nor Kennedy
have spoken to each other in a year anu
through the fortunes of the tournament
both were pitted together last night. Not
a word was uttered during the "rubber"
but both "sawed wood." Gleason was in
splendid form and he played "wrinkles'"
around Kennedy. Kennedy resorted to
the overhand "swipe," while Gleason us*>a
the underhand stroke to better advantage
Gleason "killed" the ball at every oppor
tunity and placed it with unerring aim
The first game went to Gleason by a score
Of 21-6. . :
In the second Kennedy forgot his sore
hands, wiped the fog off his glasses and
played harder. Two or three times the
score was "up-sticks" and neither player
could: gain an advantage. Gleason then
got back his winning serve and ran out
The game was a brilliant one and every
good play was generously applauded
Score:, Gleason 21, Kennedy 11.
The preceding games, .however, wero
one-sided. Henry Russ beat John Tate by
a score of 21-10: 21-11. George Littlefiel 1
defeated Tom Slevin 21-9: 21-6.
The losers will play to-morrow evening
M. Rosenfeld. and : R. . Bowman arV
matched;: John Tate ; plays "Bill" Ken
nedy; T. Slevin plays T. Kennedy, and
Thomas Walsh will play Ottie Crable.
â¢ Fred Butz,* Jack Gleason or W. Cart
wright are fancied by /the wise ones tf>
win the tournament. *
George White, a roofer by occupation
and 65 years of age, was fatally Injured
yesterday forenoon at 453 Brannan street
in front of a new building. White, with
Other men, was at work on the building
when a large beam was being hoisted In
a sling to tne second story. While tho
sling was being raised the beam slipped
from its fastening and came down with
a crash, hitting White, who was standing
under it.
The Injured man was removed to the
Emergency Hospital and there Dr. Dorr
found that many of his bones wereÂ«brok
en. Those of his left leg were broken in
three places. There was a fracture of th
right leg and besides his ribs on both sides
were broken and driven into his body The
injured man died in three hours after his
arrival in the hospital.
Tne deceased llved at 32^ Welch street.
His body was removed to the Morgu<*
where an inquest will be held to ascertain
who, if any person, is responsible for the
accident.

for the Council's Cup for
Women.
Six Qualify to Enter Third Contest
Owing probably to the heavy condition
of the Presidio course the number of la
dies who entered the qualifying, round,

"My lover's gone back on me, and I've
taken poison." H
With these words, addressed to her
brother, Florence Bell, a pretty girl of 18,
fell to the floor in the kitchen of Mrs.
Seres' milk ranch at Persia and Edin
burgh streets at 10 o'clock last night, and
died In a few minutes, having swallowed
carbolic acid.
The young woman seemed in her or
dinary mind to the occupants of the house
and no one had any idea that she would
seek her own life. Her brother, John Bell,
was standing in the kitchen when his sis
ter entered the room. She called to him,
and when he turned in response uttered
the above Â¦words, immediately afterward
falling to the floor.
Medical aid was summoned, but the girl
was beyond human aid. having swallowed
a large dose of caroolic acid. The vial
used was afterward found in her room.
The man to whom the girl referred in
her last words to her brother is connected
with the quartermaster's department at
the xresidio. A few days ago the girl,
who was deeply in love with him, learned
that he was a married man. â¢
Mrs. Seres, with whom the girl had been
living. for the last ten months, is her Bis
ter. Another sister, Mrs. W. Turner, lives
at 1017 Lombard, street, and a brother is
with the army In the Philippines. The
body was removed to the Morgue. Â¦
Die3 Because the Man. She
Loved Had Basely De
csived Her.
THE advance sale of seats for the
Root-Byers and Thurston-Cribb
fights, which are to be held at
Woodward's Pavilion on Friday
evening under the auspices of the
National Club, opened yesterday at 914
Mantet street, and the number of tickets
disposed of augurs well for the attend
ance.
That the men will enter the arena fit to
put up fast battles is evinced by the re
ports which come from their training
quarters. Jack Root of Chicago is pre
paring himself at Croll's Gardens. Ala
meda. and is already in fine fettle. His
opponent, George Byers of Boston, is
training with Charles Thurston at the
Ocean beach. Visitors to their quarters
come away favorably impressed with both
their chances of winning. Byers is said
to be very fast and extremely clever. He
Is possessed of a hard punch in either
hand and is full of confidence of beating
the clever Windy City boxer.
GIRL FALLS DEAD
AT BROTHER'S FEET
Alec Greggains, the local flght impre
sario, is so certain that Byers will win
that he has consented to go behind him
the night of the battle. Greggains is pay
ing clcse attention to his coming wonder,
"Dutch" Thurston. He boxes with the
"Flying Dutchman" every day and pro
tects his "dining apartment" with a pneu
matic pad, usually, worn by catchers in
the national game. Byers and Thurston
are down to weight and are well seasoned
for their coming fights.
Otto Crlbb, the Australian middle
weight, who is to battle Vlth Thurston, is
training at Blanken's. He assures his
friends that he will be in the ring fit to
go the route at any pace Thufston cuts
out for him. He is equally confident that
he will be returned a winner.
The curtain-raiser will be a four-round
bout furnished by Georgie Curran and
Thomas Woods. This match is a special
event. Each lad has put up a side bet of
150, and the winner of the bout is to take
the purse and side bet. There must be a
decision. Both lads are in training for
the event.
CLEVER JACK ROOT OF CHICAGO. WHO 13 SCHEDULED TO BOX
TWENTY ROUNDS WITH GEORGE BYERS. THE COLORED BOSTON
FIGHTER, ON FRIDAY NIGHT NEXT.
BY C) C. HIBBARD:
CORONADO BEACH, Jan. 14.-The ;
eighteenth annual trials of the Pacific
Coast Field Trial Club were begun on
North Island. Coronado. to-day. Eight
een dogs were run in the derby, a num
ber which has never been exceeded in the
Pacific Coast trials and has been equaled
but once in its history. Birds were found
in abundance, not packed in large bevies,
as was expected, but in small ones num
bering from ten to twenty, and in many
instances the work was done entirely on
elnsfles.
Tne day was exceedingly warm, with
not a breath of. air muvlng, rendering the
atmosphere very oppressive and leaving
apparently no scent from the btrds to
guide the dogs in their work. The result
was a day's sport with little "point" work,
compared with the number of birds
flushed by the dogs and spectators. The
cover on North Island "is too heavy in
many places to show the speed and range
of the does, while other portions, espe
cially that on the northern paxt of toe.
island, has excellent cover.
The first brace down this mornine was
Toa Sloan's pointer dog Prince OVisdom-
Wnisper; with C. E. Woraen's pointer
bucn jingle uoung Jingo-Pearl's LÂ»ot ID.
This brace was cast on. at 8:15 a. m. and
was nut long in tinaing birds. Some point
work was done by eacn, but neither wouifl
honor me point ot nia adversary. Tney
were ordered up at 8:55.
The next orace was Stockdale Kennels'
pointer bitch Jac Cuba (.Cuba of Ken
wood-Ja<julna) with C E. Wilson's Eng
lish setter dog Clipper \V (Maries Sport-
Isabella Aiaiaj. Iney were ordered on at
9 o'clock and the point was made during
ihe heat by Jac Cuba.
Clipper VV* is a handsome dog, with a
high head, very speedy and a stylish goer.
He covered his ground with excellent
judgment, but seemed deficient in nose,
oraered up at 0:37.
W. \V. Van Arsdale's Count's Mark
(Count Gladstone IV-Feach Markj and,
Ueorge Wilcox's English setter dog Stam
ford (.ritamboul-Fannie S) were the next
brace uown. More point work was shown
in this heat than in any other during the
day, as Count's Mark, after the dogs had
found birds, which was in the latter part

THE FIGHT PROMOTERS
Members of Cincinnati Chamber of
Commerce Wish to See Jeffries-
Ruhlin Bout Pulled Off.
CINCINNATI Jan. 14.â The promote-s
of the Jeffries-Ruhlin contest here Febru
ary io had their inning to-day. Ruhlin
appeared in the matinee and again to
night i and was not molested. The Evan
gelical Alliance called off their jollification
'meeting as premature and the opposition
to the fight for the first time for over a
week was not heard from. Meantime, tbe
Saengerfest Athletic Association proceeti
ed with its work at the hall and let more
contracts. But the most important devel
opment in favor of the Saengerfest Ath
letic Association was the signing of a
petition by over 100 members of the Cham
ber of Commerce during its short noon
session in favor of pulling off the fight
here as scheduled. The business men are
nearly all with the club for the fight. Un
less the political leaders give an ultima
tum against the club the fight will be
pulled off here. â¢
ST. PAUL, Jan. 14.-^Governor Van Zant
to-day received a telegram from a Cincin
nati paper asking if the Jeffries-Ruhlin
fight could be pulled off in Minnesota if
it was stopped in Ohio. He replied as fol
lows:
"Jeffries and Ruhlin will not be allowed
to fight in Minnesota. No prize fight in
the State while I am Governor."
TOPEKA, Jan. 14.â Fifteen minutes af
ter Governor Stanley was sworn into office
for his second term to-day a telegram
from a Cincinnati newspaper was placed
In his hands reading as follows:'
"Please wire us whether you would al
low Jeffries and Ruhlin to fight a limited
number of rounds in your State, in case
the fight is prevented here."
To this tne Governor wrote the follow
ing reply:
"There will be no prize fight in Kansas
while I am Governor."

Supervisor McCarthy introduced a reso
lution at yesterday's meeting which pro
vides for the approval by the Board of
Supervisors of the proposed act now be
fore the legislature to prohibit pool sell
ing and bookmaking, except within track
inclosures or buildings where contests are
carried on. McCarthy requested that his
resolution be adopted immediately under
the suspension of the rules, but it was re
ferred to the Police Committee. This
action was not taken, 'however, before the
members had listened to a vigorous
speech by Stafford In which he urged hi3
associates not to stultify themselves by
approving a rfleasure which would undo
whatever good had been accomplished by
the ordinances which, they themselves had
passed. The resolution follows: Â¦
Whereas. There is a bill pending before the
legislature of this State entitled "An act to
prohibit the felling- of pools and bookmaking
on any trial or contest of skill, speed or power
of endurance of, by or between men. animals
or machines, or upon any chance, casualty or
uncertain or contingent event, and the main
taining of places wherein poolselllng or book
making Is carried on, except when said pools
are sold or eald bookmaking Is carried on with
in the racetrack lnclosure, fair grounds or
buildings wherein such trial or contest of skill,
speed or power of endurance of, by or between
men, animals or machines shall be had and
occur, and upon the days when such trials or
contests actually occur or are had; and to pro
vide a punishment for the violation of said
act"; therefore be it
Resolved. That this board approve of the
passage of. said proposed act and earnestly re
quests the Legislature to pass the same.
During the discussion which followed
the reading of the resolution it developed
that the act if passed by the Legislature
would result in the opening of Ingleside
track. Tobin and Braunhart favored the
resolution, but expressed themselves in
favor of limiting the racing period in each
county to thirty, sixty or ninety days. It
was then that Stafford opposed the propo
sition of the board giving its support to
the measure.
"We are doing well now." said Stafford,
"for we have practically rid the com
munity of the iniquities of pool selling.
The act for which the approval of this
board is asked by the resolution will open
wide the selling of pools in this oity. I
say that under the conditions resulting
frcm such an act the city will retrograde.
"We will simply assist the promoters of
the act in inaugurating a general system
of pool selling on every contest that might
take place in any amateur or professional
contest. You are going to open the door
again and evil will result. We should not
give this measure our support. Let us
keep on in the plane we have hitherto
gene by restricting the pernicious selling
of pools."
Dwyer thought the adoption of the reso
lution would be injudicious and it was
then referred to the Police Committee.

LOS ANGELES, Jan. 14.â Damages in

before a jury.
The operation was performed about a
year ago. Bancroft was suffering from a
dislocation of his shoulder. He finally
consented to have an X-ray applied to h(s
shoulder to ascertain the exact ailment.
After the operation the flesh where the
X-ray had been applied became sore from
the burn and it continued to grow worse
instead of healing. Bancroft says he was
compelled to submit to four exposures bo
fore a satisfactory photograph had been
obtained.

MAY ELECT QUAY
ON THE FIRST BAIXOT
former Senator Seems to Have a
Majority of Both Houses in the
:Â» Keystone State.
HARRISBURG, Pa.. Jan. 14.â There Is
every indication to-night that the bitter
struggle for United States Senator will
terminate to-morrow with the election of
M. S. Quay, the nominee of the Joint Re
publican caucus. The Democrats will
vote for Colonel James* M. Guffy of Pitts
burg and the anti-Quay Republicans are
expected to distribute their votes among
several "favorite sons."
The House and Senate will vote sep
arately at 3 o'clock to-morrow afternoon
and the probabilities are that Mr. Quay's
combined vote will not fall short of 130.
It requires 127 votes to elect and It is
pretty generally conceded that he will poll
at least this number. Should Mr. Quay
or any other person receive the requisite
number of votes to-morrow the Senate
tfind House will .meet jointly at noon
Wednesday to canvass the returns and
formally declare an election.
There is nothing to indicate to-night
that there, will not be an election on the
first ballot or that there will be another
deadlock similar to that of 1899, when the
Legislature adjourned after taking seven
ty-nine ballots without making a choice.

HARDING DIVORCE SUIT
BEGINS AT SAN DIEGO
Millionaire Combats His Wife's Mo
tion for Money to Defray At-'
torneys' Fees.
SAN DIEGO, Jan. 14.â The divorce suit
of George F. Harding, the Chicago mil
lionaire, against his wife, was up in the
Superior. Court to-day, on a motion for
$1000 attorney's fees and $500 costs of suit
for Mrs. Harding. Victor .. M. Harding,. a
son, was present in behalf of his mother,
who Is in Florence, Italy, an invalid.
When questioned en the witness stand
he estimated his father's wealth at from
$1,000,000 to $3,000,000, and said that his
gross income from this ought to be $120,000
a year.
On the other hand the father, who is
70 years old, testified that his property
was lncumbered ' for $800,000, and that if
he were compelled to sell it at the present
time he would find himself still in debt
for a quarter of a million dollars.
Regarding his mother's â¢ wealth the
younger Harding said she had nothing
save the $300 that Mr. Harding paid her
each month in accordance with a decree
of an Illinois court and that from this
sum she had to provide for three daugh
ters living with her.
Mr. Harding; Sr. estimated his wife's
wealth at about $30,000. Twelve years ago,
he said, he gained access to a safe deposit
box where ehe kept her securities and he
found therein stocks and bonds having
a par value of $28,000. He thought she still
had nearly all - of .It, though he had no
means of' knowing.

Special Dispatch to The Call
STOCKTON, Jan. 14.â There is no doubt
in the minds of the peace officers that a
murder was committed in the southern
part of this county early this month. The
body of the victim, an unknown young
man who could not have been over 25
years of age, is now in the Morgue. The
body was discovered by Constable Gieseko
of Tracy. The dead man was lying on his
face in a ditch at the side of the railroad,
between Paradise Cut and the bridge
across the San Joaquin River. The left
hand lay under it and the right arm was
outstretched. The skull was crushed in
in several places. The body was garbed
in underclothes and, a shirt only. The
garments had been expensive. About
twenty feet away a Knox hat was found
with a puff tie folded In the crown.
An autopsy was i performed at the
Morgue to-day and it was found that the
man s skull had been crushed in four
places, evidently with some blunt instru
ment. There was a round hole in the
forehead, which looked as though it might
have been made by a blow from the
rounded head of a mechanic's hammer.
There were similar holes In the left tem
ple, In tho top of the head and in the back
of the head. The body had been covered
by the overflow water from the river and
may have lain there,- the Coroner says, a
week or more.
The deceased was 5 feet V^ inches in
height, had brown hair and brown eyes
and weighed about 140 pounds. The only
clew on which the officers have to work
is the laundry marks which were .found
on the underclothing. The jury at the
inquest returned a verdict of murder.
STEAMER GUATEMALA
SAILS FROM PANAMA
Her Departure Marks the Opening of
a Competing Steamer lane on
the Western Coast.
Special Cable to The Call and New York Her
ald. Copyright, 1901. by the Herald rub
lishins Cothnany.
PANAMA, Jan. 14.â Tho Pacific Steam
Navigation steamer Guatemala, Captain
Harris, left for San Francisco Sunday and
will touch at Central American and Mexi
can ports, inaugurating, the company's
new service from Valparaiso to California,
jointly with the Panama railroad and
South American steamship companies, In
competition with the Kosmos line from
Hamburg and the Pacific Mall from Pan
ama.
The Pacific Mail has no intention of
withdrawing from the field, which it has
monopolized for about nfty years, and
will continue running on the coast, offer
ing to take freight and coffee, especially
from Central America, and giving equal
facilities to European ports by way of San
Francisco and the Southern Pacific Rail
road in time equal to that of the Isthmus
route. The greatest competition ever
known on the coast is now fully estab
lished. It will be advantageous to Cen
tral American and Mexican coast trade
especially.
The Royal Mall steamer Atrato, which
left Colon on Saturday, carried $600,000 in
gold. This is said to have been shipped
in Cartagena for England by a prominent
Colombian general.
. The American warship Philadelphia an
chored here this morning at noon and
fired a salute, the shore batteries answer

FINDS MURDERED
YOUTH'S REMAINS
Tracy Constable Discovers
Evidence of Brutal
Crime.
DENVER. Jan. 14.â The joint caucus of
the fusion members of the Legislature to
night decided to support Hon. Thomas M.
Patterson of Denver for. United States
Senator, to succeed Senator Edward O.
Wolcott. There are 91 fusionists in the
Legislature, Including Democrats, Popu
lists and Silver Republicans, and of these
S7 were in the caucus. Mr. Patterson re
ceived 74 votes on the flrst ballot.
The public was surprised early this
evening by the announcement that ex-
Governor Charles S. Thomas, who has
been Mr. Patterson's, leading opponent,
had withdrawn from the race. His name
did not go before the caucus. The total
vote of the Legislature on joint ballot
is 100.
Thomas M. Patterson was born in Ire
land November. 4, 1840. With his parents
he came to America in 1S53. After a short
residence in New York 'the family re
moved to Crawfordsville, Ind., where
Thomas worked for some time in a print
ing office. He was educated in Asbury
(now Depauw) University. He enlisted at
the first call for troops, but served only
a few months on account of poor health.
He read law and practiced his profession
for a time in Indiana. In 1872 he came to
Denver and formed a partnership with
Hon. Charles S. Thomas, who, it ia inter
esting to note, was his leading opponent
in the Senatorial contest just closed. This
partnership lasted a year, but was re
sumed in 1879 and continued until 1890. In
1674 Mr. Patterson was chosen Territorial
Delegate to Congress and greatly aided in
securing "statehood for Colorado. In 1876
he was sent back to Congress, this time
as a Represntative. At the end of his
term Mr. Patterson resumed the practice
of law in Denver. In 1890 he purchased a
controlling interest in the Rocky Moun
tain News, to the conduct of which he has
since very largely devoted his time. In
1S92 Mr. Patterson was a delegate to the
National Democratic Convention. In which
he made a strong fight for the adoption of
a free silver plank. Being unsuccessful he
led the bolt against Cleveland, which car
ried the State of Colorado for Weaver. In
1896 he was a delegate to the Populist Na
tional Convention, which indorsed Bryan,
and in 1900 he was permanent chairman of
the convention of that party. It is under
stood that he now considers himself a
Democrat. - . .
Mr. Patterson in 1S63 was married to Miss
Kate Grafton, a granddaughter of Alex
ander Campbell, the founder of the Chris
tian church. They have one daughter,
Mrs. R. C. Campbell of Denver.

Special Dispatch to The Call
SAN JOSE, Jan. 11.â The charges of
arson and murder growing out of the
burning of the home of Conrad Ruff and
the death of four little children were dis
missed by Justice Rosenthal this after
noon. Sensational developments were ex
pected at the preliminary examination of
Mrs. Sallie Ruff, the stepmother, accused
of having started the lire that caused the
children's death, and the courtroom was
crowded, but the evidence introduced was
very tame.
Mrs. Ruff was present with her attor
i<ey and her husband and hfs three broth
ersâJonas, Ludwig and Carl Ruffâ and
William Valdez, the hired man, all of
whom had in retaliation been charged
with arson by Mrs. Ruff, were interested
spectators. .
William Valdez was the first witness in
Mrs. Ruffs examination on arson. He de
scribed the fire and could give no idea as
to how It started. Louis Ruff, one of the
children that was rescued from the burn
ing building, said the room was so full of
smoke he could hardlv breathe when
taken out of the window. Carl Ruff, ths
complainant, believed Mrs. Ruff had set
lire to the house because she was not ex
cited afterward. He said he swore to thj
warrant because he believed his sister-in
law was going to leave this city and take
the insurance money with her. Mrs. CieU.
a sister of Carl Ruff, was the last witness,
but knew nothing connecting the defend
ant with the conflagration. "X
District Attorney Jarman then asked
the court to dismiss the charge of arson
against Mrs. Ruff. Justice Rosenthal
said no evidence had been presented to
sustain the charge and he doubted that
an^ crime had been committed. He then
ordered the dismissal. This was followed
by a dismissal of the murder charge
against Mrs. Ruff and those of arson
against Conrad, Carl. Jonas and I/udwig
Ruff and William Valdez.
All the arrests seems to have resulted
from a quarrel over the $1000 insurance
money. This had been made payable to
Carl Ruff by Conrad Ruff, and his wife
stopped its payment. The insurance com
pany has watched the cases closely, but
it is not known what action it now will
take in regard to nayment.

delegates arrived during the' day. The
attendance will be further augmented by
those who are to arrive to-night and on
the early trains to-morrow morning.
It Is safe to say that nearly every prom
inent stockman west of the Missouri
River will be present by to-morrow morn
ir.g. :
A larg-e delegation Is present from Chi
cago and a fair attendance from other
points east of the Missouri River.
The famous Pueblo Cowboy Band ar
rived this afternoon and was met at the
depot by the local committee and escorted
through the prinicpal streets of the city.
The executive committee of the associa
tion held a meeting this afternoon and
fliscussed In a general way the subjects
to be takon up by the convention.
Secretary Martin's report was read and
adopted. Th<? report is substantially a
history of the association since its incor
poration.

Post, Corps and Circle.
Since the Legislature is to be asked to provide n
remedy for the new evil, let us hope that care will be
taken this time to avoid the former mistake, and that
the new remedy shall be so devised that its operation
in the complex affairs of modern life will not be worse
than what we havr. now. ?
By reason of these things many thousand acres of
bnd have already passed into the possession of the
State; and of course, unless some, safe and easy
method be provided for disposing of them, v the
amount of land in the hands of the State will event
ually become a serious evil. '
We are having an illustration of this lack of fore
sight in the operation of the statute enacted to pro
tect delinquent taxpayers from the old system of sell
ing their land for taxes. The remedy adopted for
what was thought to be an evil is that of having the
State take possession of land on which taxes are un
paid, giving the delinquent owner five years in which
to redeem his property by paying the taxes due, to
gether with interest upon them. It is a good remedy
for the evil it was aimed at, but unfortunately in some
respects it is worse than the evil itself, and the Legis
lature is to be asked to remedy the remedy.
It appears that under the. operation of the present
law the State is rapidly; becoming a, very large land
owner. When taxes upon a lot of land oncebecome
delinquent the charges and the interest run up so
lapidly that before the five years are out in which the
owner has a right to redeem they exceed the value
of. the land. Under such circumstances the owner
naturally refuses to redeem, and it is said that an out
sider would be foolish to buy it because j)f a possibly
defective title. . ~
r\ LMOST any man can. perceive an evil and de
/^ termine something of its nature; a considerable
* number can go so far as to devise a remedy for
it, .but they are few, indeed, who can foresee how
the remedy will affect other things in the body politic
and know beforehand whether it will be better or
worse than the disease it is designed to cure.
A REMEDY THAT FAILED.-
This marvelous accomplishment has been attained
first by the thorough education of the people of the
State, second by an industrial energy which has made
full use of every manufacturing and commercial pos
sibility, and third by a moral and mental culture
which has prompted the people t*o aspire to the hiizh-
The New England States are not rich in natural
resources and Boston^ has no monopoly of Atlantic
coast commerce. Nevertheless in the savings and co
operative banks of Massachusetts alone there is stored
up one-fifth of the savings of the American people.
One-thirtieth of the population of the United States is
within fifty miles of Boston, and one-twentieth of the
national wealth is held and one-thirteenth of the
clearing-housp business of the country is done within
fifty miles of the New England metropolis.
In all such blowing on the part of Boston there is
nothing for us or for any other portion of the people
of the United States to complain of. The rule laid
down by Joshua at the siege of Jericho is applicable
here: "When I take the trumpet and blow, then take
ye your trumpets and blow also." If any other city
has done as well, the air is free for blowing and the
country' will be glad to hear the sound of it.
The matter is of more interest than that of a passing
pews item of the time. It is worth while for the peo
ple of San Francisco to consider "how Boston .attained
all her wealth in order that a profitable lesson may
be drawn from her example. â¢
Population, however, is the least of the claims of
Boston. It is asserted that her superiority in wcaltK
is much greater than her superiority in numbers, and
that within a radius of fifty miles she has the "best
purchasing community in the world." It is pointed
out that: "The census shows that on a basis of full
and equal valuation Boston is the richest city in the
country, with an average of $1942 for every inhabi
tant, while New York has a per capita wealth of only
$1337; Philadelphia $1127, and Chicago $1016." It :s
added that while the average earning capacity of the
men, women and children of the United States is
onry 40 cents a day, the average for the people of
Massachusetts is 73 cents, or 80 per cent above th'e
general level of the country as a whole. Finally, the
information is derived from a commercial agency in
New York 'that of the $35,600,000 distributed from
that city in interests and dividends at the beginning
of the year, upward of $20,000,000 was assigned to
Boston,, leaving but $.15,000,000 for the rest of the
Union. Â¦ '
The claim of Boston is that she has within a ra
dius of fifty miles a laiger population than any other
equal extent of territory in the United States with the
exception of New York. According to the figures
of the last census there are within the B'oston district
2,849,000 people; in the Philadelphia district 2,545,000;
in the Chicago district 2,167,000. Thus while Boston
holds the fifth place in the list of American munici
palities, it is the center and the market place of the
second most populous territory in the country.
BOSTON, as was to have been expected, has met
the new century by blowing horns metaphorical
rather than literal. Instead of contenting her
self with an uproar in her own streets, she has waited
t'ntil the racket of the occasion in other cities has
died away, and now she is blowing a great trumpet
blast in honor of herself and her glory.

ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
AAYOR PHELAX made of his banquet to the
officials of the city on Saturday evening an
occasion for renewing his slurs upon che
press. Ignoring the courtesies expected of a host at
such gatherings, he went out of his way to be dis
courteous, and exposed the fact that he has during
his term of office learned as little of good manners
as he has of good politics.
The rudeness of hi; words was the more marked
by the fact that he adopted his slurring tone toward
the press in calling for an address from Mr. Dohr
mann, president' of the Merchants' Association, his
evident intent being to convey the implication that
Mr. Dohrmann shared his sentiment. That intent was
completely foiled by Mr. Dohrmann, who in begin
ning his speech stated emphatically as a clearly un
derstood rebuke to the Mayor, "Gentlemen, I am a
friend of the press."
The Mayor's slur consisted in a reference to whit
he called "an interested press, or the interested edi
tors who sit in high towers and whose mission is so
exalted that they cannot get down to the level of the
common people."
Just what the Mayor meant by sayir.g the press
"cannot get down to the level of the common peo
ple" is hard to say. It has certainly managed at times
to get down to his level long enough to- note
what manner of political muck and mire he
lias -been dabbling in ever" since he was in
vested with the large powers conferred by
the new charter upon the chief executive of the
city. It lias from time to time noted the manner in
which he and his satellites have distorted the plain
purpose of the civil service provisions of the char
ter and converted the municipal" government into a
patronage bureau of the worst kind.
Mayor Phelan entered office under what seemed tc
be conditions favorable to good government. It ap
peared upon the surface that he had been elected by a
non-partisan vote and that he was free to administer
municipal affairs under the new charter fairly and
honestly. In a very short time after the charter went
into operation, however, it was seen that he was
bound to the worst elements in the city. Instead of
reform in any direction there has been a movement
backward and downward. His administration began
with a scandal over the Police Department and has
gone on steadily from bad to worse". He is now so
utterly discredited that he couia not be elected to
any office whatever. His downfall dates from the
iss'ue of The Call of January 16, 1900.
For the loss of his former credit in the city he
blames the press. Because it does not descend to
his politics and that of his g?ng he grows sarcastic
and charges it with being above the level of the coni
cion people. A< a matter of fact, its only of
fense to him is that it does not descend to the level
o* the toadies upon his official power who have flat
tered his head until it is swollen with vanity and be
trayed his feet into prths that have led him to do
lineal ignominy.

Morocco holds a most important strategic point in
North Africa, and neither of the great powers has
been willing to see it pass into the hands of another.
It appears, however, that the "psychological moment"
has come at last. The nations are agreed. France is
to give up her rights in Newfoundland and Great
Britain is to give up all opposition to France in
Morocco. What concession has been given to Ger
many does not appear, but doubtless the Kaiser has
been satisfied in some" way, and so the new century
begins with another of those bargains by which great
nations make friends with one another at the expense
of tlie weak.
It is not clear what claim the British have in
Morocco, but it is quite certain that neither Great
Britain nor Germany was willing to permit France
to annex any considerable portion of the territory of
the Moors. Consequently the advance of the French
in that direction was suspended. The situation, ho/w
ever, has continued critical cer since. The experience
of the world has been that when once a nation be
gins to conquer weaker nations and take possession
of their territory the expansion must keep on. Great
Britain found it so in India. Russia lias found it so
in Central Asia. Germany will- find it so in Africa
as soon as she begins to develop her possessions in
that continent. When the civilized man comes "into
contact with the barbarian or the savage it is impos
sible to draw a line beyond which there shall' be no
aggression.
Of all the partition schemes the great nations have
undertaken at the expense of the weak this is perhaps
the oddest. For a long time the French have been
looking with longing eyes upon a portion of Morocco
bordering the French province of Algeria. Once or
twice there have been conflicts along the line, and
readers with good memories may recall that last year
there was a little war scare in Europe brought about
by the extension of a French railroad toward the
oasis of Twat, in the desert of Sahara. The best way
for the road to reach Twat was across territory be
longing to Morocco, Â«nd, moreover, Morocco herself
claimed the oasis. So there was prospect of war, and
diplomatists were busy in every capital in Europe.
self, for it is territory belonging to' Morocco.
It now appears that a sufficient consideration has
been found; that at last Great Britain has a price to
pay which France is willing to accept. It is not a
money price. France would scorn that. It is a con
cession of territory many hundred times larger and
richer than the barren shore France claims in New
foundland. It happens! moreover, to be territory,
which Great Britain can concede without loss to her-
Far away back in the .eighteenth century a treaty
was made between the British and the French whereby
the fishermen of France were to have certain im
portant privileges not only in the Newfoundland
fisl^eries but on the Newfoundland shore. Those
privileges have fretted the people of Newfoundland
for many a year, and time after time. an earnest peti
tion has been sent to the British. Government to put
an end to them. The French, however, have clun;;
to them persistently â more, perhaps7from sentimental
than from industrial reasons. It is the last vestige,
of their former great possessions in that part of the
â¢world, and woe would have befallen any French
Government that surrendered it to Great Britain for
any consideration whatever. S

tions with the French Foreign Minister, M. Delcasse,

tween the two nations concerning tile. French rights
in Newfoundland.

Under our system of law the relations of attorney
and client must of necessity be based upon confidence
on the one side and integrity on the other. There
nre at the present time in the United States interests
amounting in value to- many millions of dollars rest
ing solely upon the word of lawyers. Something like
commercial chaos would ensue if the bar should fall
below the standard of honor established in the past,
and to which all clients trust when they seek counsel.
The number of scandals in this city resulting fro*r;
dishonesty on the part of attorneys has become a re
rious injury to the community. It is time for the Bir
Association to take 'action. "
Finally it is charged that Watt, still acting as at
torney for Mrs. Underhill, undertook to look after
her interests in the Latham Company in Chicago,
and while he was in that city obtained from her the
sum of $30,000 ostensibly to maintain the Latham
Company and so protect her interests in the firm,
but up to this time has never given his client an ac
counting of what he did with the money, notwith
standing the fact that the Latham Company went into
dissolution.
âSo far as appears from the record the only defense
made by Watt is that all claims Mrs. Underhill has
against him are offset by his services as attorney.
Can any honest member of the Bar Association
doubt that such charges should be investigated?
It is further charged that while Watt was acting as
attorney for Mrs. Underhill, she being in New .York
and he in this city as her agent and legal adviser, it
became his duty to remit certain sums of money to
her, hut instead of remitting the money or valid drafts
he sent her three checks drawn upon the American
Eank and Trust Company of this # city, each and all of
them being valueless, so that when presented for pay
ment they were dishonored and returned unpaid.
It is to be borne in mind that the charges against
Watt are not vague and indefinite. He is specifically
charged with having given to his client, Mrs. Under
hill, ?.s security for money borrowed from her, two
mortgages, each of which had been satisfied and dis
charged long before Watt made use of them as a
pretended security for his loan.
Under these circumstances about the only way in
which Watt can be tried for the alleged dishonesty
toward his client is for the Bar Association to sum
mons him to answer the charges and investigate them
impartially but thoroughly. The public has a right
to expect the association to perform that duty.
In one important respect the duty of the associa
tion in the Watt case is much more imperative than
in that of Chretien or in that *of Rogers. Those of
fenders were in the hands of the courts held to
answer to charges of crime. It-jvas therefore pos
sible for the Bar Association to make the specious
pica that those cases should be left to the courts alone.
No such plea can be advanced as a reason for ignoring
the charges in the case that has now been brought to
light. James Alva Watt has not been summonsed to
answer at the bar for crime, nor can he be sum
monsed to answer in that, way for the particular of
fenses charged, since they were committed so long ago
that the statute of limitations prevents prosecution.
ONCE more the Bar Association of San Fran
cisco is on trial before the bar of public
opinion. Nothing was done by the association
to redeem the legal profession in this city from the
stain put upon it by the conduct of Chretien and of
Rogers. It now remains to be seen whether any
thing will be done to investigate the charges made
upon good evidence against the professional honesty
of James . Alva Watt in his dealings with his client,
Mrs. Fannie A. Underhill, as published in The Call on
Sunday morning.
A DUTY OF THE BAR.
Members of the Cabinet receive $8000 a
year, and the seme amount is paid to the
Vice President of the United States. Rep
resentative Loi;denslaBer of New Jersey
has introduced a bill increasing the sala
ries of the Cabinet officers to $15,000 and
that of the Vice President to. $25,000. It
Is a bill that ought to be adopted. The
history of the United States is full of in
stances where the services of brilliant
men have been lost to the country be
cause of the failure of Congress to pro
vide adequate compensation. The social
conditions of high official life in Wash
ington are such that few men can go into
the Cabinet unless they have independent
fortunes. The salary does not begin to
pay the expenses. A President must,
therefore, as a rule, make up his Cabinet
from among the wealthy classes, and
therefore he Is restricted. The only fault
we find with this bill is that it does not
go far enough. It should include the Em
bassadors. Only rich men can represent
this country in I-ondon, Paris, Berlin or
St. Petersburg. Out of their salary they
must rent expensive housos and must
meet their own expenses. There are fÂ«>w
more extravagant places than St. Peters
burg, and it is very seldom that a Minis
ter has remained at the Russian court for
a full term. Not only should our Embas
sadors be better provided for In the way
of salary, but in the great capitals the
Government should own the buildings oc
cupied by its representatives. â Philadel
phia Inquirer.
If the mission of a newspaper Is to
right wrong, the Boston Herald is entitled
to congratulations upon having accom
plished it. David L* Stain and Oliver
Cromwell, two men long past middle age,
were confined in the Maine State prison
on !ife sentences for a murder committed
eleven years apo. Six months ago evi
dence came into the possession of the
Herald which showed that a doubt existed
as to the guilt of the prisoners. The pa
per instantly set an Investigation on foot,
followed it up at its own expense, and
finally found conclusive proof that these
men wer/ suffering punishment for a
crime committed by other persons. The
proof was laid before the authorities, a
pardon secured, and on the day after New
Year's Stain and Cromwell were released
from prison.â Chicago Journal.
Salaries That Should B<* Increased.
The total railway mileage of the United
States is 190.000 miles. Of that over 45,080
miles is owned by roads whose policy 19
controlled at this time by J. Pierpont Mor
gan by virtue of his own holdings and the
confidence reposed in him by English
bondholders and by certain Americans
who are large holders of railway securi
ties. E. H. Harriman and his associates
control railroad systems, .chiefly west of
the Mississippi, which have a mileage of
over 24,70") miles. There are roads which
are not subject to the control of either of
these railway kings, but whose managers
approve of their policy. The Pennsylvania
and Santa Pe a"re the more important of
these outside roads. The object of this
concentration of railway control in a few
hands is the prevention of rate cutting. â

A Newspaper's Mission.
The Earl of Hopetoun was inaugurated
Governor General of the new common
wealth of Australia January 1. This was
the final act in the proceedings that vir
tually transformed the six British colonie3
of New South Wales, Victoria, Queens
land, South Australia, Western Australia
and Tasmania into a federal republic.
The six colonies became States in the new
federation, each reserving all rigrhts not
delegated in the constitution of federation
act to the National Government. It Ja
significant that in organizing a scheme for
national government the Australians fol
lowed the American rather than the Cana
dian plan as to State autonomy and as to
Senate and House.â Chicago Inter Ocean.

EDITORIAL
UTTERANCE
IN VARIETY
Massachusetts early learned the value of manufac
turing enterprises, and of the technical schools which
fit young men to engage successfully in carrying them
on. She has had to bring from a distance nearly all
of the raw material upon which her factories worked.
In California we would have a great advantage over
her in that respect, for our natural resources are
abundant. It is, then, worth our while to see if we
cannot, surpass her in the future. We have a better
climate, more abundant water power and are equally
as well situated for commerce.' Why not beat Boston?
cst attainable level of life and to work steadfastly to
achieve it.

Supervisors Acted Unwisely.
The Supreme Court yesterday handed
down a decision reversing the Judgment of
the Jowor court In the case of J. "W.
Stumpf vs. the Board of Supervisors of
San Luis Obispo County. It appears that
the board neglected to examine a petition
presented to it to find out whether or not
certain petitioners were freeholders of the
district, and declared that the Templeton
Sanitary District was not a legally or
ganized district. Stumpf brought suit as
a taxpayer and the lower court held that
the order made by the Supervisors was
valid. The Supreme Court holds that the
order was Invalid.

BE THE GUEST OF HONOR
â¢Chamber of Commerce to Tender a
Reception to the Distinguished
Railway Official.
President C M. Hays of the Southern
Pacific Company will be the guest of
honor at a reception this afternoon by the
Chamber of Commerce of San Francisco.
The distinguished railroad official will be
formally, greeted by the members of the
chamber and kindred organizations. The
reception will be held at 3 o'clock and
President Hays will be escorted to the
rooms of the organization by George W.
McNear Jr. and Wakefield Baker.
Preceding the rewption the annual
meeting of the Chamber of Commerce will
be held and the annual reports will be
presented. William M. Bunker, who late
ly returned from abroad, will deliver a re
port on "The certainties of our trade with
Asiatic Siberia." There Is e%*ery likeli
hood of a large attendance at the meeting.
STOCKTON MAN WANTS
DAMAGE FROM RAILROAD
W. H. Shannon Claims the Southern
Pacific Has Infringed Upon
Patented Guard Bail.
\V. II. Shannon of Stockton has begun
suit for damages against the Southern
Pacific Railroad Company for alleged in
fringement on a patent which Shannon
claims to have been granted him five or
six years ago. The device Is Intended for
use where railroads cross each other at
right angles. Before the idea was con
ceivedMhe crossings were bolted down to
a bedplate, with which they were so thor
oughly incorporated that whenever any
one of the four corners was badly worn
the whole crossing hud to be torn up.
The invention consists mainly of rectan
gular pieces, acting as gxiard rails at each
corner of the crossing, and they are so
adjusted that In making repairs a great
deal of time, labor and expense Is saved.
According to Mr. Shannon all the rec
tangular crossings on the Southern Pa
cific, Santa Fe and other roads are pat
terned after his device, consequently he
intends to collect damages for alleged In
fringement. Photographs have been taken
of every crossing in the Southern Pacific
system, and these will be used as evi
dence when the case comes to trial. The
action ngainst the railroad will involve
many thousands of dollars. -

Edward J. Morris, formerly a hackman,
appeared before Judge Conlan yesterday
on a charge of grand larceny. He was
accused of stealing Dr. Mark Newman's
fox terrier Sunday afternoon. Detective
T. B. Gibson' had occasion to call at Dr.
Newman's house. 253 Turk street, and
when the door was opened to admit him
the dog ran out. Morris happened to be
passing at the time and he picked up the
dog and walked away with It. Dr. New
man followed him and asked him to give
up the dog, but he refused, claiming it
was one he had lost. The doctor - called
Gibson and ordered him to place Morris
under arrest. Then 'Morris wanted to
"cut It all out," as he expressed it, but
tho doctor would not have it. The c^ae
was continued till Thursday and- Morris
was released on $100 bonds, accepted by
the Judge. The dog was sent home with
Dr. Newman.
