235=the square root.
Explanation: Point off the number into
periods of two figures each, beginning at
the right. Find the. largest possible num
ber which when squared will divide the
first period. 3 squared is 9 and is too large.
2 squared is 4 and will divide the first
period, which is 5. Place the 2 as divisor
at the left and ln the quotient space at
the right. Divide, subtract and bring
down as in long division, only two figures
(a period) are brought down Instead of
one. Now we change the quotient each
time. The new quotient is found by
doubling the answer thus far secured. 2
double cive3 4. 4 into 13 (omitting the 2)
win go 3 times. Place the 3 in the answer
space and also after the 4 in the divisor.
Now multiply. 3 times 43 equals 129. Sub
tract and bring down as before. For a
new divisor double the 23 and we have 46.
This divided Into 232 (omitting the 5) will
go 5 times. Place the 5 in the divisor and
multiply. Then 235 is the required square
root.
Exercises.
Find the square root in each Instance;
1. 3025 6. 11664 11. 4C36
2. 9604 7. 21025 12. 5184
3. 196 8. 1S225 13. 16CS4
4. 629 9. 471*1 14. 41616
5. 17Â»i6 * 10. &C25 15. 11025
Noteâ The student can prove the correctness
of his answers by squaring them. For instance,
we 'know that 15 is the square root of 225 be
cause 15 times 15=225.
XXXâ HOW TO MULTIPLY DECI
MALS.
Multiplication of decimals should be ar
ranged in the manner indicated ln the fol
lowinsr examples:
Â« 'J Multiply 3.6S04 by 25.13.
3.68 O4 ' Arrange the decimals
23.13 one under the other so
that the decimal points
73.60 8 may be in tho same col
18.4O 2O umn. BegiD the multl
.36 8O4 plication with the left
.11 0412 hand figure of tho mul-
tipllerâ that is, with the
92.4U18452 2; but since to multiply
by 2 in the tens' place
is in reality to multiply
by 20, the result must be written one place
to the left cf its natural position, the
decimal point being kept in the same
place as those above it. Then multiply in
succession by the 5. X. 3; at each stage
writing the result one place to the rl^ht
of that last obtained and preserving the
column of decimal points. Lastly, add the
partial products as in ordinary addition
of decimals.
The advantage of this arrangement is
that it lends Itself to contracted multipli
cation when only an approximate result
is required. ,
In this example all the figures to the
right of the vertical line except one col
umn could be omitted and yet the product
would be the same to two decimal places.
A thorough knowledge of decimals Is a
necssary foundation to satisfactory work
In mathematics.
XXXIâ HOW TO DISTINGUISH
"WORDS THAT ABB OFTEN CON
FOUNDED.
â¢ (Supplementary Paper.)
BY PROFESSOR H. G. BUEHLER.
Carry, Bring, Fetch â To carry means "to
lake along in going"; to bring means "to
take along in coming"; to fetch means "to
go, get and bring"; as. "What shall I
bring you from Paris?" "Harry, please
fetch a chair from the hall"; "Farmers
carry their potatoes to market."
Character, Reputationâ Character is
what a man is: reputation is the prevail
ing opinion of his character; as, every one
admires the character of Washington";
"A distinguished general may lose his
reputation through a single blunder."
Claim, Assert. Allejre. Maintain. De
clare, Affirm, State â To claim means prop
erly "to demand as one's own or one's
due." It is often looselv used, especially
in the United States, for "assert," "al
lege," "maintain," "declare" or affirm."
To assert is "to say or declare ln the face
of implied denial or doubt." To alleee is
"assert without proof." To maintain 13
to "uphold by argument." To declare is
to "say publicly, clearly or emphatically."
To affirm Is to "assert on one's reputation
for knowledge or truthfulness." To state,
which is often loosely uted in the sense of
"say," "assert," "allege," "declare" or
."affirm," means properly to express form
ally and in detail.
Complement, Compliment â A comple
ment is a "full quantity or number" or
"that which Is needed to complete": a
compliment is "an expression of praise";
as, "The ship . has its complement of
stores"; "Present my compliments to your
father."
Continual, Continuousâ Continual is used
of frequently repeated acts; as, '-'Contin
ual dropping wears away a stone": con
tinuous, of uninterrupted action: as "The
continuous flowing of a river." v ":.*-
Custom, Habitâ Cuslom denotes the fre
quent repetition of the same act. and may
be used by a number of persons taken to
gether. Habit is the effect of custom in
a person. Custom i3 voluntary: habit is
involuntary, often uncontrollable, some
times unconscious. The following sen
tences are correct: "Dancinc round a
May-pole is a custom many hundreds of
years old"; "A loose life brings a man In
to habits of dissipation."
Deadly, Deathlyâ Deathly, in the sense
of "resembling death." as "She wa3
deathly pale," is prererable to deadly,
since deadly also means "inflicting
death"; as, "The bite of some insects is
deadly." ' Â¦
Different From, Different Toâ Different
from is preferable to aifferent to and dif
ferent than. .
Drive, Rideâ We go driving in carriages,
riding In saddles. We drive bei-.nd horses;
we ride on them.
Dumb. Stupidâ Dumb properly means
"mute " "silent." Its misuse for stupid Is
partly due. especially in Pennsylvania, to
its resemblance to the German dumm.
Emigration. Immigrationâ Emigration
moving out from a country; Immigration,
the moving into it. Foreigners who come
to live in America aro emigrants from
their fatherland, immigrants to America.
Funny, Oddâ Funnv means "comical";
in the "sense of "strange" or "odd" it id
not in good use.
Grand Gorgeous, Awful, Etc.â Grand
properly Implies "grandeur"; gorgeous

â I. e., brilliant luster"; lovely, "of sur
passing loveliness": m&gnlflcent, "grand
in action or appearance." â¢
Emerson, in his essay on "The Superla

would lead you to suppose that they had
lived in a museum where all the objects
were monsters and extremes. â¢ â¢ â¢ They

superlatives are diminutives and weaken.
? â¢ > â¢ All this come3 of poverty. We are
unskilled deflners. i From want of skill to
convey quality, we hope to move admira
tion by quantity. Language should aim
to describe the fact. â¢ â¢ â¢ 'Ti3 very
wearisome, this straining talk, the?e ex
periences all exquisite, intense and tre
mendous."
Hire. Let. Lease â To hire means "to ob
tain the use. of"; to let. "to give the use
of." To lease means "to give the u?e of
by lease." The.owner of a house leases it;
the person who occupies it takes a leas*
of It.

It is necessary truaimost all mechanical
work that the stutAnt should understand
how to find the square root of large num
bers. The square, of a number is the
product of the number by Itself; as. 25 is
the square of 5, 16 is the square of 4. The
square root of a number is the number
which multiplied by itself will produce
the number; as, 5 is the square root of
25; i2 is the square root of 141.

Home means one's habitual abode, â¢'the

"may not be in a house, and it may include
the surroundings of a house:
How â How properly means "in what
manner" or "to what extent." It is often
misused for "that" to introduce an object
clause.
In. Intoâ In implies presence inside of.
or within; Into implies movement to the
Inside of. Before a man can move in a
room, he must already have moved into it.
Invent. Discoverâ We invent something
new; contrived or produced for the first
time: as. "Galileo invented the tele
scope. We discover what existed before,
but remained unknown; as. "Xewton dis
covered the law of gravitation."

I leave that to your own judgment."
Locate. Findâ Locate properly means "to
place in a particular position." or "to
designate the site of," as of a new build
ing or purchased lands; it does not mean
to find. It is correct to say. "Where will
the hetf schoolhouse be located?" It Is
not In accordance with the best usage to

man."
Mad, Angryâ Mad means "insane"* in

Materializeâ To materialize properly

Mutual. Commonâ Mutual properly
means "reciprocal." "interchanged."* It

The .following sentences are correct:

"I find. Miss Helen, that we have some

l"Â«ear, Nearlyâ Near Is an adjective; tha

Proposal. Propositionâ A proposal f*
something proposed to be done, which
may be accepted or rejected. A proposi

Quite. \ eryâ Quite properly means "en

Real, Really, Veryâ Real Is properly an
adjective, meaning "not imaginary or
counterfeit"; as, "real diamonds." Its
misuse for the adverbs really and very,
as. "This is real pretty." Is a vulgarism.
Some, Somewhat, Somethingâ Some is
an adjective; as, "Some water"; "Some
brighter clime." Somewhat is an adverb;
as. "He is somewhat better." "Some
what" is occasionally used as a noun; as.
"Somewhat of doubt remains," but In this
sense something is more common.
Start, Begin, Commenceâ To start is "to

tiv<*, "begin" or "commence" is used. Be
gin is preferred in ordinary use; com

and ceremonial.
Transpire. Happen â To transpire means

tice," "to leak out"; It should not be used
in the sense of to happen.
When. While â When means "at the time
that"; while, "durins the time that," "as
long as." When fixes attention on a data
or period; while fixes attention on the
lapse of time.

Tivoli.
"Cinderella," on again this week at the
Tivoll, is a glitter of wit, songs, costumes
and settings, and of all the firefly dance
by the electric girls is the crowning fea
ture. Here la a dance â an electric ballet â
that Kuropean or Eastern bills have not
yet announced. It is a San Franciscoâ a
Tivoliâ production and its merit is- as
great as is its brilliancy. Four pretty
girls draped in scintillating robes of fire
appear in the dance. Hidden In the folds
of the costume of each are fifty-six 8-can
dle power electric lights, while eight 1
candle and one 16-candle power lights dec
orate the head dress of each dancer. In
all 260 lights, 3000 feet of wire and twelve
circuits are utilized in producing the ef
fect. This is the reward of six months'
constant experiment and the result well
repays the time and energy expended.
.From other places have come those whose
claims to fame have been builded on the
splendor of the scenes amid which they
danced, but at the Tivoli, in "Cinderella,"
new ideas have been set to more brilliant
Bcenes.
Hartman produced new jests last even
ing and toward his example his support
leaned with pleasing result.

Four elephants of assorted sizes are the
attraction at the Orpheum this week. This
is a novelty in vaudeville, but then any
thing is likely to happen in vaudeville.
The sagacious beasts do wonderful things,
displaying an intelligence almost human
and nearly e<iuÂ»l to that of a press agent.
They dance and play a variety of instru
ments, including the mouth harmonica,
the cymbals, the hand organ and the bass
drum. The bicgest of the four plays
ninepins with skill, and his partner marks
with chalk on a slate the number of pins
bowled over. But the most sensational
feat of all is the riding of a tricycle by
the baby elephant. Billy Link, the new
mcnolosrist, comes to the front with
some jokes that, while brand new in
Gotham, are gray and bald with age in
San Francisco. He has a laugh, however,
that is as infectious as the smallpox.
Eugene O'Rourke and Rose Braham ap
pear in a sketch entitled "Parlor A,"
which has' all the furniture and stage set
ting for a side-splitting affair, but which
is disappointingly tame. 'Gene and Rose
make the most of the situation and by
clever acting save a poor sketch from fail
ure.* There are several interesting and
amusing views in the biograph with which
the entertainment closes. The holdovers
take as well as ever, notwithstanding the
absence of vocal music.
Orpheum.
The unfortunate persons engaged in
presenting: this atrocity to the public
have as much mistaken their profession
es has the Grubstreeter who deserted his
native mud to woo his bedraggled Muse
in the glare of the footlights. The actors
of this company cannot act, the singers
cannot Einjr. the dancers cannot dance.
Have they forgotten that there are dishes
to be scrubbed, railroad ties to be laid,
stones for turnpikes to be broken? Let
them betake themselves to these useful
and honorable occupations and thereby
cease to insult the public by calling them
selves members of the dramatic profes
sion. Â¦ .
The melancholy performance presented
last night at the California Theater can
not be classified under any known dramat
ic category. It is neither farce, burlesque,
extravaganza, parody, pantomime, fish,
flesh, fowl nor good red herring. It is
like the earth in pre-Adamite timesâ
without form and void. It has neither
rhyme, wit, humor, satire, sense nor non
sense. Compared to "A BreÂ«zy Time,"
a Broadhurst farce is a masterpiece. The
force of a comparison can no further go.

It Is to weep .this week at the Central
Theater, a nice comfy weep, without any
fear of the pearly tear congealing upon
one's youthful countenance. For the new
steam heater Is a distinct success, not to
mention "The Two Orphans," and the
only apparent danger now is that one's
liquid emotion may go up in steam. When
the snow It snowed last night (and who
ever heard of an orphan fit-out without a
snowstorm?) nary a shiver struck the per
spiring audience. It had troubles of its
own and would willingly have taken a
turn under the falling Hakes itself, as it
surreptitiously mopped its brow and took
off its coat. No, there's no chance of a
shiver up at the Central this week and
those who are looking for one must go
forth and find elsewhere.
I Yesterday was a field day at the Pre
sidio for the representatives 6f the rail
road companies, as 1C2 volunteers were
discharged. Previous to the discharge of
these men the railroad companies had
combined, and a representative from the
Southern Pacific, the Panta Fe and the
Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company
handled the business. The soldiers upon
payment of the price of a ticket were
given orders for tickets that entitled them
to transportation over any of the lines.
The business was divided on the per
centage plan. Thl3 was evidently unsat
isfactory to the Oregon line people, for
yesterday they broke away from the other
people and sold tickets independent of the
combine. A merry war was thus inaug
urated, as the Southern Pacific and Santa
Fe representatives telephoned to the main
offices for reinforcements. The soldiers
relished the competition, although the
war did not result in any change of rates.
Paymasters Kelleher, Kilbourne and
Wilklns paid off the men, many of them
receiving a considerable sum, as the re
sult of from a year to eighteen months'
service in the Philippines.
Lieutenant A. M. Davis, Fourth Cavalry,
has been made captain and commissary.
Ihe promotion makes Captain Davis a
staff officer. Fred L. Sprague, sergeant.
Company I, Twentieth Infantry, died yes
terday at the General Hosaitu.1.
The bodies of seventy-two soldiers that
arrived on the freight transport Califor
nian were taken to the Presidio yesterday
and stored, awaiting burial or shipment
east to relatives. The remains are those
oi men who died or were killed in China.
Ground was broken yesterday at the
General Hospital for the erection of an
assembly hall and chapel. These improve
ments have been In contemplation for
some time, but work was delayed until
the arrival of the necessary permission
irom the department. â¢ The assembly hall
will be used by the soldiers for dances,
concerts and various entertainments held
heretofore in the mess hall.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time of
day. tho third time column gives the last tide
of the day, except when there are but three
tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are In addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey charts, except when a
minus sign ( â ) precedes the height, and then
the number given is subtracted from tho depth
given by the charts. The plane of reference
Is the mean of the lower low waters.

A man named Jim Sweeney telephoned
to thÂ« Carmen Island Salt Company yes
terday representing himself to be a mem
ber of a prominent wholesale firm and
saying that he would send a man down
for a load of. salt. He then drove down
to the company's wharf and had loadea
up his wagon when one of the clerks be
came suspicious and rang up the firm
which Sweeney claimed to represent. All
knowledge of the affair was denied, ana
Sweeney was arrested Just as he was driv
ing off with the load. He was. taken to
the harbor station, where a charge of
grand larceny was placed against him.

afternoon on Twenty-fifth and Howard
Streets by Sergeant Blank and Police Of
ficer Taylor of the Seventeenth-street Sta

been stolen out of a wagon. The win<Â»

Vineyard and was on Us way for delivery

Healy Btopped at a grocery store on

streets and while absent the barrel of
wine w'an abstracted from the wagon.- Ho
reported the loss at the Seventeenth-street
Station just as the night watch was re
porting on at 8 o'clock. Sergeant Blank
and Police Officer Taylor were detailed Â»Â»y
Lieutenant Anderson to Inquire Into th^
theft. 1 and they Intercepted Brannon, wh:>
had the wine in a wagon hurrying along

Milan used to be connected with the
Alpha Trained Male and Female Nurses
Agency, of which his slater is the man
ager. The agency is at 1117 Van Ness ave,
hup, a few doors north of the Ward resi
dence. Milan gave himself up last night
and was placed In 1ail.
Miss Ward saitf that she had known
Milan for years. He had been pestering
her with his attentions, and when he
would meet her on the street he would
insist upon accompanying her. Friday
evening he met her on Van Ness avenue,
and because she positively refused to have
anything to do with him he struck her.
breaking her hat and bruising her face.
She thought he was a fit subject for ex
amination by the Insanity Commissioners.
She had borne his persecution ao long that
she was determined to stop it.

LOS ANGELES. Jan. 14.â Los Angeles'
present law affecting: municipal franchises
is, according to the best authorities, In
valid, and it has been recommended that
the City Council grant no franchises un
til it is repealed. \V. S. Hook of the Los
Angeles Traction Company worked hard
before and during the district and State
conventions to pledge delegates in favor
of a new law which would enable the
Council to award franchises to the high
est bidder. Hook has allied Interests di
rectly with the Terminal Railroad, and
consequently with Senator Clark. Hook
and Gibbon. Clark's agent and attorney,
then worked to obtain control of the new
City Council, nnd believed they had got
It. Hook accordingly a week ago filed pe
titions for four franchises, and was Im
mediately met by the Los Angeles Rail
way (Huntlngton's company) following
their example. The Los Angeles Railway
is now evidently anxious that the new bill.
Hook's original Idea, should be passed.
RAILWAY BACKING
FRANCHISE BILL
California Miners' Association was pre
sented In the Assembly by Assemblyman
Irish and in the Senate by Senator Tyrrell
of Nevada this morning. The document
is explanatory of bills already Introduced,
providing for the appropriation of $150,000
additional for the construction of Im
pounding and restraining dams In the
hydraulic and placer mining region. Fol
lowing is the petition:
The California Miners' Association, through
Its executive committee, begs to call your at
tention to the following facts:
1. In 1893 the Legislature of this State appro
priated $250,000 to be used in the construction
of works for the restraining and impounding
of debris resulting from mining operations,
natural erosion and other causes.
2. This appropriation was not to become
available until the United States Government
should have appropriated at least an equal
amount for the same purpose.
3. In 1S96 Congress appropriated J230.000 for
the above purpose.
4. After a most careful and painstaking ex
amination and study of the matter the Fed
eral engineers submitted their plans for the
contemplated work.
5. These plans involve an expenditure of
$SO0.0OO Instead of $a00.000, leaving a deficiency
of $300,000 to be made up by the State and
National Governments.
6. Through the emergency river and harbor
bill of June 6, 1900, Congress made provision
for the immediate commencement of the con
templated work and its prosecution to the ex
tent of the then available State and National
appropriations, amounting to a total of $300.
000. To this provision. In the emergency river
and harbor bill of 1900 there was added a
proviso to the effect that one-half of the cost
of the contemplated work should be paid by
the State of California.
7. The plan of the Federal engineers for the
entire work. Involving an expenditure of $800,
000. has been approved by the State Board of
Examiners of this State, and bills looking to
the appropriation by the State of California of
the further sum of $150,000, as its final contri
bution to the contemplated work, have already
been Introduced In the Senate and Assembly.
These bills are known as Senate bill No. 127
and Assembly bill No. 4<J, and were Introduced
by Senator Tyrrell of Nevada and Assembly
man Irish of Sierra.
8. It is well understood that further appro
priation by Congress is contingent upon tl>Â«
action of your honorable bodies.
We therefore most respectfully and earnestly
request early and favorable action at your
hands upon the measures above noted.
E. C. VOOHHEIS,
President California Miners' Association.

A. A. Baroteau was called and asked
whether he had taken Lady Yarde-Bul
ler from the house where she was living
in Oakland up to the middle of November,
and had gone with her to Alameda and
thence to San Francisco.
The "Baron" denied the charge, but ad
mitted that he was at the house for some
time before her Ladyship went away, and
that they happened to take the same car
to Alameda and also went on the same
boat to San Francisco In the morning.
"Did you not go with her to a notorious
house In Alameda?" asked Fitzgerald.
"I did not."
"Did you not admit having done so in
conversation with me? Remember, you
are under oath."
"Honest, I never did."
"Your Honor," said Mr. Fitzgerald, "I
am utterly at a loss to comprehend this
answer."
The hearing will go on to-morrow.
On cross-examination Chapman wanted
to know whether she was insane all the
time.
Chapman at once objected to the ques
tion. Judge Ellsworth ruled that it was
permissible only if Fitzgerald proposed to
submit proof of Insanity. Fitzgerald de
clared that this was just what he pro
posed to do.
The witness answered that he thought
his mother had been insane for the last
two years. He testified that she was en
tirely under the Influence of any one who
attempted to control her for any purpose,
even strangers having" no difficulty In
persuading her to sign notes and guar
dianship papers. She had told him that
her property was worth Jll.fflO.flOO and
wanted to (jo to England to secure prop
erty which exists only in her imagination.
Arthur Blair confirmed his brother's
testimony and told of his mother's coming
to San Francisco, where he lives, about
two months ago." In Baroteau's company.
The next morning after they arrived he
was informed by telephone that she wn*
lost and did not know where to go. He
found her alone In a saloon In the west
ern part of the city and Intoxicated.
He argued that the case should be dis
posed of at once as Baroteau had filed a
petition for letters of guardianship In San
Francisco.
Lady Yarde-Buller appeared In court
heavily veiled and attended by her maid
and "Baron", Baroteau.

Wheeler.

Resolved. That wa most resqectfully request

JOHN PARTRIDGE.

Secretary-Treasurer.
Now, therefore, the printers of San Francisco
do resolve that we protest against the continu
ance of said printing office in opposition to
legitimate private business, and as an un
necessary expense to tha taxpayers of the
State.
Whereas, The State Printing Office Is run
entirely at the expense of the taxpayers, pay
ing no rent. "Insurance. Interest on investment
or any other expanse such as has to be paid on
private business, and still It costs infinitely
more to have work done there than any pri
vate printer In the State would charge for tha
same work.
Whereas. The furnishing of the State Print
ing Office has cost the taxpayers of the State
hundreds of thousands of dollars, all of which
Is now claimed to be worn out and useless,
and a demand is to be madn upon the Legis
lature now in session to make an appropria
tion for the refurnishing of sai<l printing of
fice, all of which will be. useless again In a
few yearn; and
Whereas, Tho State of California In now en
gaged in the printing business. In competition
with the regular printers and taxpayers of the
State; and
The Employing Printers' Association cf
California at a meeting held yesterday
discussed the State Printing Office at Sac
ramento. As a result the following reso
lutions were adopted and will be forward
ed to the Legislature, now in session:

C. G. CALKINS,
Lieutenant Commander, U. S. N., In charge.
celebrated its first anniversary last night
and honored the advent of the Greek new
year in a general reunion and banquet at
Druids' Hall, on Sutter street. This club
is now perhaps the most influential so
ciety of its kind in the city. It has more
than GOO active members, and is organized
not simply for political purposes, but for
the improvement, encouragement and ad
vancement of Its members, among whom
are numbered some of the most Influential
members of the local Greek colony. J
The banquet last night was in every
way a success. The members and guests
were welcomed by President N. Valianos,
who delivered In Greek an address which
was frequently applauded. The speaker
congratulated "the club upon the progress
which It had made, and urged the mem
bers to greater efforts of union and or
ganization.
A. G. Kosta, the vice president, also
spoke briefly but eloquently upon the need
of organization. Speeches were made by
E. Antoniades, Alex Feros and M. D.
Vanvales. O. C. Pratt, the legal adviser
of the club, complimented the members
upon the progress which they had made
and counseled them to further efforts In
their own behalf. During the ' evening
Greek instrumental and vocal music was
rendered. â¢

Lieutenant Colonel French of Chicago
will succeed to the command of this de
partment. He will arrive on the 22d and
will be given a reception.
Lieutenant Colonels William and Rich
ard Evans and their wives were tendered
a magnificent farewell banquet by the
Salvation Army at the barracks on Mar
ket street yesterday evening preparatory
to their departure for the East, whither
they go to assume command of the de
partment about Boston. The occasion
will long he remembered in army circles,
but not without a tinge of regret, for the
departing commanders were very popular
among their subordinates. They had been
at the head of affairs in this division for
over three years and were known in near
ly every town of the State.
Delegations from the towns about the
bay were present to take part in the pa
rade, which was preceded by the head
quarters band, and the street parade waa
the most pretentious of its kind witnessed
for several years. - '
During the course of the banquet many
touching speeches expressing regret at the
i departure of the officers and commending
their work in this State were made. A
tumultuous scene of enthusiasm occurred
when Colonel Williams arose to thank his
comrades.

The steamer San Jose was In a bad
plight yesterday. Coming down from Port
Costa she got a hawser. Into her propeller
and that caused a stoppage of the ma
chinery. Luckily the steamer was near
to her berth at the Mail dock, so the an
chor was dropped. A diver will be sent
down to-day and he will cut the rope off
close to the hub of the propeller. Then
the portion that has become entangled
In the blades will be allowed to wear off.
The steamer will not be delayed and will
get away on her regular sailing time.

m. yesterday. There is a dense fog out
side and vessels will not attempt to come
in. The arrivals were the Newark from
Bowens Landing and Sequoia from Fort

Water Front Notes.
The chances now seem to be that .the
British ship Andrada has gone ashote
somewhere on the coast of Vancouver
Island. She left Santa Rosalia on Novem
ber 12 for Oregon and arrived off the Co
lumbia River about three weeks ago.
Pilot Cordiner was put ashore and then
a gale came up and the Andrada was
driven to sea. Since then nothing has
been heard from her, and seafaring men
fear that she was the vessel sighted bot
tom up off Flattery.
The Andrada was a sister ship to the
Andelana and Andorihana. The former
capsized in Tacoma harbor two years ago
and all hands were drowned.. For ten.
months she had lain at anchor awaltinsr
a charter, and just when the contract was
signed and the work of "stiffening" had
begun, she turned turtle during a storm
and went down.
The Andorihana was in the fire that
did so much damage to New York's water
front last year and was almost totally
destroyed. She has been refitted and will
be out here next year. The "Air" line
seems to be playing in hard luck and its
friends are. stilt hoping that the Andrada
will turn up.
as Among the Missing.
EUREKA, Jan. 14.â The steamship Po
mona came Into port to-day with the for
ward part of her guard rail torn off. a
portion of her cabin near the social hall
smashed in and one of her boats broken
in as a result of a collision with the
schooner Fearless about 4 o'clock yester
day afternoon. The schooner was lying
at anchor near Duxbury Reef and a
heavy fog prevented the officers of the
Pomona from seeing the schooner until
the steamship was too close to avoid a
collision. Captain Shea sheered -off as
eoon as he discovered the schooner. This
action resulted in hitting- the Fearless a
glancing blow instead of striking her
amidships as \ ould otherwise have been
the case. On account of the lateness of
the arrival of the Pomona, Agent Baird
has been compelled to change her sailing
time from 7 a. m. to 3 p. m. This acci
dent makes the third the Pomona has
met with within a month. The first was
while she was crossing the bar December
16, when a wave broke in her cabin. The
second resulted irom a severe windstorm,
which forced her on January 3 to take
shelter at the Farallon Islands.

The cases were submitted together, as
they involve three points: That the pro
ceedings in relation to the issuance of the
bonds not having been completed when
the charter went into effect, the Board
of Supervisors had no power to proceed
to issue bonds, as the charter intervened;
secondly, that the bonds in both cases
were invalid because there was no spe
cific declaration of the result of the elec
tion; the third question Is confined to the
panhandle case. The appellant contends
that the proposition to condemn for pub
lic use these Feparate pieces of property
should have been submitted as one thin?,
and that a notice of election for the pan
handle bonds should have specified that
the bonds were payable in a specific kind
of money â gold coin of the United States.
Mr. Fritz was represented by Arthur H.
Rarendt and Charles E. Naylor. Garret
MeEnerney, City and County Attorney
Franklin K. Lane and Assistant City and
County Attorney G. W. Lane represented
the city and county. Briefs in favor of
the bonds were filed by Joseph M. Roths
child. D. C. Murphy and J. C. Chetwood.
In the McHugh case, the attorney for the
appellant is William T. Baggett and the
city and county is represented by Attor
neys MeEnerney, Lane and Lane.
There were two cases bearing on this
vital Question and they were combined in
the arguments, as the questions involved
in each were alike. One of the actions
was that of Eugene N. Fritz against the
city and county of San Francisco, which
involves the validity of the panhandle
bonds, and the other la the suit filed by
Kellx McHugh apraiiiFt the city and coun
ty, which involves the validity of the sew
ers, schoolhouse and hospital bonds. In
both cases demurrers filed by the City
and County Attorney were sustained and
judgment rendered for the city. Appeals
were taken by F.ritz and McHugh and ar
guments were heard yesterday.

cases which are being heard by the Su
preme Court Fitting In bank. Mayor Phe
lan was an interested listener and other
city and county officials strained their
ears to hoar the reasons Bet forth by
counsel for and against the Issuance of
the bonds.

BERKELEY, Jan. 14.-Editor F. M. Al
len and Manacer F. E. Reed of the Call
fornian, the student papÂ«r, are having?
trouble with their former publisher. B. F.
Arnold, the manager Â»f the Gazette Pub
lishing Company. Yesterday Arnold had
everything in readiness to print the Cali
fornian. He 'left the staff of the college
paper in charge of his office and when
he returned the members of the staff had
disappeared, taking with them all of the
advertising, cuts from, their paper. To
day the 'Callfornian made its appearance
from an Oakland printing office." -
Last Ausust Arnold made a year con
tract with J.-W.-S. BullÂ«r,- then manasrer
of the Callfornian. It was under this con
tract that he expected to print tue col
lege paper this term.
"We do not wish, to make 'any trouble
for the. boys," said Mr. ~ Arnold, "but we
believe we could hold them under the con
tract. - I -have consulted a lawyer, but
nothing will -be done until , Butler returns
to college next week.".
Editor Allen declares that the cuts tak
en belonged, to the staff. and they did not
exceed their risht. Tho members. of; the
staff acknowledge that they have broken
their contract with Arnold, but they hold
that he has \ done ' the same thinp ; many
times before.

1118 Broadway, Jan. 11.
The Home for the Adult Blind at Te
mescal is now In better condition than for
many years, and if the present Legisla
ture srant s- the modest demands of this
Institution Superintendent banders ex
pects to have an actual surplus to the
credit of the factory In place of the $S0O0
deficit which he found there when he took
charge less than two years ago. During
Mr .Sanders' former term he had a fund
of $5000 to the credit of the factory, all
made from- the profits of broom-making.
When Mr. Sanders took charge he found
that there was a deficit of $3000. The last
Legislature met $6000 of this deficit, and
Mr. Sanders will ask the present Legisla
ture lo make up the balance, in or
der tRat the Institution -may start even.
No deficit has occurre.i during the present
management, and the $2000 appropriation
to be asked for will bo applied upon old
bills contracted under a former adminis
tration. Of these bills 11631 27 is for mer
chandise furnished the Home and $470 40
is for salaries.
In order that the shop at the' Home
might be opened and work provided, Su
perintendent Sanders secured broom corn
to the amount of $3450 upon his own per
sonal credit. This was donÂ© In order to
save the increasing 1 market value of the
corn to the Home. Superintendent San
ders will also ask the Legislature to ratify
this action, and pay for the corn, as It has
resulted in a great saving for the State.
Broom Corn_ Purchased Upon Per
sonal Credit Tliat Will
Save Much, to the
State.
Legislature Will BÂ» Asked
to Meet an Old
Deficit.

TAKEN TO A MISSION
AGAINST HER WILL
Young Chinese Woman, Dragged
From Her Home, Declares Police
Acted Without Warrant.
Quite a little excitement was Â» caused
among the Mongolian residents of Sulli
van alley shortly after 5 o'clock yesterday
afternoon by the forcible carrying off of
Tew Tow, a handsome young Chinese
woman from her home. Corporal Anthony
and Policeman Ben Kaskell, who Is bailiff
in Judge Conlan'e ' court, entered the
woman's room and told her she must go
with them to the Washington-street mis
sion. When she objected the offlcerÂ» took
hold of her and tried to force her along.
The woman lay on the floor 'and made a
strong resistance, crying to the men to
show her a warrant for her arrest and
promising to go peaceably If they pro
duced authority.
Yee was finally taken from the hou3e,
but when she reached the street she fought
Â¦ vigorously, kicking and biting- the bfflcer3
in her vain effort to release- herself. To
her friends he declared that the officers
were kidnaping her and were acting with
out warrant. She said that she was 20
years of age and was being taken from
her home agratnst her -win. All the way
to the mission she fought and struggled,
yelling at the top of her voice and de
nouncing the action of the mission peo
ple and the unlawful methods of tha po
lice. The woman was taken to the mis
sion, where she kept up an incessant pre
test. No record of her arrest was made
at the City Prison. .

AND SPLIT FOLLOWS
Many Walk Out of Meeting at Which
! j;*^~ -Â¦'. It Is Decided to Form a .
/ .. Union.
A union or not a union was the per
plexing problem which was troubling the
brains of the fair milliners of this city,
and it proved a rock upon- which they split
last night. They are unanimous in their
desire to have the milliner stores close 'at
6 o'clock, but some of- them hesitate and
become extremely ".timid when | they see
the. possibility- of a dreadful strike loom
ing up before them. At the meeting last
night the matter was discussed in a very
stormy manner. Â¦ .
Two ; factions arose â the unionists and
the , non-unionists. The former agitated
the : imm"edlate formation, of. a union and
affiliation with the federation of unions,
Â¦whlle-the latter thought the storekeepers
would accede to- their demands without
the necessity of taking any such drastic
measures. The unionists, who were well
organized " under the \ leadership - of Mrs.
Reynolds,'. finally, carried. their point, and
the'lr opponents withdrew from tho meet-
Ing Â¦'-'A union was formed and Mrs. Bessie
Collins was elected president. J. D. Pierce
and. wife of the American. Federation or
Labor 'were present and addressed the
meeting on the alms and advantages of
the labor union. Â¦ :

THE SLAYER, HIS WIFE AND THE VICTIM IN THE TRAGEDY WHICH
WAS KNACTED YESTERDAY AFTERNOON IN THE KITCHEN OF
THE WINDSOR HOTEL.
The Supreme Court opened its January
calendar yesterday and listened all day to
arguments in many now celebrated cases.
The appeal; of Cordelia Botkin. the
wpman who .was convicted Â¦ of poisoning:
Mrs. John P. Dunning and her sister, was
arguAl and submitted. Attorney General
Tirey.L. Ford appeared in behalf of the
people and replied to the address de
livered by Charles Heggerty, counsel for
appellant. The argument of Mr. Heg
gerty was ' â¢' devoted solely, to the point
that there was an insufficiency of evidence
to warrant a conviction. The Attorney
General confessed error in this case, as he
did in the Brandos case, which also came
up for hearing.
When the Brandes case was called by
Chief Justice Beatty, the Attorney Gen
eral admitted that error had been com
mitted" by the lower court.' Attorney F.
W." Sawyer of Oakland, attorney for the
man/ who was convicted of choking and
beating his 14-year-old daughter to death,
Informed the court that the evidence was
insufficient to convict his client. He said
he hoped to secure a dismissal of the case
by the lower court and thereby save the
county of Alameda flvo. or six thousand
dollars for prosecuting another trial.
'The "case was' ordered placed at the
foot of to-day's calendar."

his victim and opened fire on him at close
range from behind a door without a word
of warning. The fact that no weapon was
found on the dead man bears out the
theory that it was a brutal, premeditated
murder. The motive for the crime is j
somewhat obscure, but from remarks |
dropped by Glaze may have been fancieo
insults offered to Mrs. Glaze.
O'Connor, when first questioned by the
police shortly after the murder, pretend
ed to know nothing of the shooting, but
when taken before Captain of Detectives
Seymour last night admitted that he wil
ressed the shooting and then made the
following statement, explaining that his
assumed ignorance when first, questioned
was because he feared to talk:
"I was on duty in the afternoon," said
O'Connor, "when I answered a call to room
27 and was asked to get some drinks from
the bar. The bar is situated up a flight
of stairs in the interior open court of the
hotel. I went to the bar and the bar
keeper told me that he had no limes and
requested me to go and ask Mr. Trewella
for some. The barkeeper told me that
Mr Trewella had just gone downstairs
with Mr. Glaze. I went to the kitchen,
which Is right under the barroom, and
saw Mr. Trewella inside.
"I did not see Mr. Glaze around. I told
Mr. Trewella what I wanted and he asked
me to go with him to the storeroom and
he would give me the limes. Mr. Trewella
vrent ahead and I followed him from tho
kitchen. There are three steps which
lead down from the kitchen to the level
of the interior court and the storeroom
is opposite the kitchen door.
"As Mr. Trewella had just started down
the steps of the kitchen the first thing I
knew was that Mr. Glaze was near the
steps and was firing at Mr. Trewella. I
was so scared that I cannot remember
how many shots were fired. I know that
as Mr. Trewella sank to the ground Mr. j
Glaze was still firing his pistol.
"Then Glaze went into the bakery and
went right around the kitchen and came
up behind me, having gone through an
other door. He said to me, 'If any one
asks you how he was shot, you say that
he shot himself." Glaze then walked away
and I tried to help Mr. Trewella up, but
I W2.s unable to do so. Then I went into
the hotel and soon afterward the police
and doctors arrived."
Trewella and Glaze had been in part
nership for ten months, both men be
ing married and having stepchildren. Tho
two families resided in the hotel. As far
as is known, Trewella and Glaze hav^
been on most friendly terms, and the
shooting came as a surprise to all the at
taches of the hotel.
A few minutes before 2 p. m. yesterday,
Trewella and Glaze wore scon in the lobby
of the hotel, apparently engaged in friend
lj* conversation.
The two men proceeded to the rear of
the lobby and passed out into the Interior
open court of the building and ascended
to the saloon, which is situated over the
kitchen and bakery of the hotel.
After taking a drink in the saloon, Tro
wella and Glaze went downstairs and en
tered the bakery, which adjoins the kitch-
In a few minutes the sounds of pistol
shots startled the guests and attaches of
the hotel, and several of them saw Glaze
as he was walking away from the body
of his victim. He appeared cool and calm
ly ejected the exploded shells from his re
vclver and started to reload the weapon.
As he entered the office of the hotel. Al
bert Shelley, Glaze's stepson, who, was on
duty, wrenched the revolver from his
hand and cursed him. He then told him
to give himself up to the police. Glaze
did as he was told, and surrendered- to
Police Officers N. J. Barry and D. J. Drls
coll at the corner of Eddy and Market
streets.
"I have shot my partner, Trewella.,
said Glaze to the officers, to whom he was
known, "and I want to give myself up.
We have often had trouble about domestic
and business affair? and he threatened mo
many times. A little while ago he came
toward me in a threatening manner and
made a motion as if to pull a weapon. I
thought he had a gun, bo I pulled my pis
tol and shot him."
Glaze was taken to the Southern station,
from which place he was conveyed to the
Hall of Justice.
As soon as the shooting was known to
the employes of the Windsor -Hotel, ur
gent calls were sent for physicians, as
Trewella was still aUve. Dr. F. A, Mc-
Mahon, 230 Turk street, and Dr. G. W.
O'Donnell responded. They hastily exam
ined Trewella and found that he was be
yond human aid, as two of the bullets had
entered the stomach. While the doctors
were cxamlnine Trewella the wounded
man gave a gasp and <Qied.
The newsof the shooting spread rapidly
and a large crowd congregated in front
of the hotel, requiring the presence of a
number of policemen to keep the sidewalk
clear. The Coroner's deputies soon ar
rived and searched Trewella's body, but
no weapon was found on it, a few papers,
$2 in money ajid some keys being all that
was in the pockets of the dead man.
The policemen and Detective Cody ex
amined the pistol with which the shootinjr
had been done and- found one new car
tridge in it. A careful search of the bak
ery revealed the presence of five empty
Phells, and It is evident that Glaze, after
firing all the bullets from his pistol, had
ejected the empty cartiidges and put in
a single shell. Just why Glaze did this he
declines to state.
Soon after being conveyed to the Hall
of Justice, Glaze sent for his attorney,
I. I. Brown, and for Mrs. Glaze. He sat
in the lobby of the prison and talked
earnestly with his wife and his attorney. ;
"I had to kill that man," said the pris
oner to a reporter for The Call; "that is all
I can say. I know that is a serious state
ment to make, but either I had to kill
him or he would have killed me.: I cannot
say any more now, as my attorney has
advised me to say nothing. This is a
serious position for me to bÂ« In. wÂ«rÂ« I

IN KITCHEN OF THE WINDSOR HOTEL
SAYS CHINATOWN
IS THE SOURCE
OF MUCH EVIL
Rabbi "v'oorsanger AdvccatiS
Ita Removal From the
Limits of City.
Municipal League Appoints Commit
tee to Ask Police Commissioners
to Concentrate Celestials
in One District.
The subject of the "social evil waa
taken up again last night for discussion
by the Municipal League. The Rev. Dr.
Voorsanger, who has made a careful
study of the matter, gave it as his opin
ion that Chinatown Is the great moral
cancer of tne city.
"If it was possible to remove China
town," said he. "San Francisco would be
the most moral city on earth. With this
plague spot removed we would have but
little to complain of from the standpoint
cf morality. True, there would be other
evils to contend with, but they could be
regulated. The luxury of our modern
times has too strongly operated on u e
imaginations of our poor. Hunger Is the
grcau!3t ar.aichlst in the world. When
hunger Ftall:s abroad those who do not
wish to die sell themselves for bread.
"We must treat this evil with great ptty
for the victims of the social conditions
we have created. Treat it as we may, I
will never agiee to licensing that which
}s evil. I am opposed, bitterly opposed
to licensing any evil, for no government
hus the right tc make money out of that
which morality forbids. I am opposed 10
licensing objectionable houses the same as
I am opposed to high license as a rem
edy for the liquor traffic. It is wrong
to sell liquor, the Government has no
right to license the business at all. To
licence any of our social evils, is Just aa
bad as if we were to take hush money.
If a thing is wrong, let us condemn it,
but never make it a source of revenue for
the soveriiiutui. 1 am not in favor or
drastic iiicasuits. however. 1 will never
consent to making object lessons of Â«ie
unfor^jn ii'i women in our midst, unt 1
the gambling houses on Ktarny street,
the pawnshops on Market street ana tae
gilded palaces and underground dives are
removed. The great is that our
business men like theories, and become
frightened almost to death when you
present them with facts. As the first step
toward removing the social evil, 1 favor
the condemning of Chinatown and Its re
moval to a site selected near Lake MerÂ«
ced. I am more afraid of Chinatown than
of our fallen women, drunkards and gam
blers. We can deal with the latter ,
classes, but it seems from present out
looks to be impossible to deal with China
town. Let us first remove Chinatown and
then we can easily deal with the rest."*
Irving Davis, who followed, spoke at
length on the evils that existed, both be
fore and after the Parkhurst crusade in
New York City.
Isidor Jacobs favored the concentration
of the evil in ono section of the city.
Rev. Dr. Scott agreed with Dr. Voor
sanger in many of his views, but did not i
think that the removal of Chinatown |
would noticeably purify the moral air of
the city.
"I do not think it practical to remove
Chinatown," said Colonel J. C. Currier,
"for so many influential people are de
riving revenue from It that any such
project must meet with strong opposition.
I believe that the classes complained of
should be put in one section of the city
and quarantined."
"The evil can bo minimized," said
Supervisor Braunhart, "but to what ex
tent depends upon the honesty of the of
flcialb. In the past the unfortunate crea
tures have been subject to blackmail.
They have- been blackmailed by officials
elected to execute the laws. I do not
think it right to license the evils of the
ojty and that is what it practically
amount3 to If arrests are to be periodical
ly made, with the understanding that tho
offenders are to pay light fines*. If our of
ficials not accept bribes and levy black
mail, but instead carry out the lawsnow
exacted, we will soon remedy the social
At the conclusion of the discussion the
league appointed Messrs. Jacobs. Rea.
Schlesslnger. Greensfelder and Henderson
to appear before the Police Commission
ers and endeavor to bring about arrange
ments to concentrate the evil in a section
of the city set aside for that purpose.
Messrs. Schnee, Selfridge, Well, Salfield.
Davis, Greensfelder and Rea were ap
pointed a committee to appear before the
Supervisors to-morrow afternoon and
lend their moral support to .the proposed
ordinance fixing the railroad fare' for
school children at two and a half cents.
Before adjournment the league on
motion of Isidor Jacobs adopted the fol
lowing, resolution.
Whereas. There is pending before the Legis
lature bills to provide the necessary revenuw
for the proper maintenance of our State Unt- |
verslty. in accordance with its rapidly growing I
requirements; be it j
Resolved by the Municipal League of San j
Francisco, That we heartily Indorse the said I
measures and ask their passage by the State
Legislature, bo that the University of Califor
nia may not be retarded or hindered In carry-
Ing on its work so necessary to the cause of
education in bur State.
